## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:47:29)

_Thought for 5s_

Here is the full state of the attack as I currently understand it.

The headline is that the two-point / derivative-geometry work has not produced a final closure, but it has compressed the live space dramatically. The geometry is no longer the main obstacle. What remains is increasingly package-level, relational, and possibly nonlocal/provenance-sensitive. turn65file1

## 1. Global decomposition of the live lanes

The live two-point problem now separates into three conditional routes.

The first is the **strong finite-order source-level / package-level merger route**: prove the actual corrected two-pair cubic/quintic/septic package has the right merger/functoriality on small two-atom data. The second is the **weaker exact quotient-diagonal route** on the residual exact fixed-shear corner, where the branch has been compressed to a quotient-visible transport problem. The third is the **mixed \(\lambda\)-shear / projective-shadow route** on good overlap patches, which is where the local cancellation-chart analysis lives. The old naive source-summed corrected-block route is no longer regarded as viable; the even-amplitude audit killed the hope that it would provide the needed linear merger law. turn65file1

At the same time, the whole overlap geometry is now much more sharply split. On a good overlap patch, any close exact mixed two-point bad branch can accumulate only on
\[
\{S=0\}\cup\{K_v=0\}.
\]
Inside that, the derivative-geometry work has split the possibilities into:
\[
(K_v=0,\ S\neq 0)\quad\text{mixed low-order regime,}
\]
\[
(S=0,\ K_v\neq 0)\quad\text{transfer to nonmixed 4-point geometry,}
\]
and only
\[
\boxed{\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\}}
\]
remains as the genuinely residual derivative-geometric corner. That is a major structural gain. turn65file1

## 2. What is now genuinely established on the exact fixed-shear corner

On the residual exact fixed-shear slice, the local branch is involutive / quotient-diagonal. After centering and reparametrization, the projected germ has the form
\[
\Phi(t)=(t^m,G(t)),\qquad m\ \text{even},\quad G(-t)=G(t),
\]
so
\[
\Phi(t)=\Phi(-t)=\widehat\Phi(t^2).
\]
Thus the local exact repeated-value branch is no longer a free two-sided local branch; it descends to the quotient variable
\[
u=t^2.
\]
This is one of the strongest theorem-level gains from Attack B. turn65file1

The associated transport quotient
\[
Q(t):=\frac{J_{u_5}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)}
\]
is even:
\[
Q(t)=\widehat Q(t^2).
\]
Writing
\[
\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}=2t\,\widehat{\mathcal D}(t^2),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
and setting
\[
e:=\ord_0\widehat{\mathcal D},\qquad
r:=\ord_0J_\lambda=\ord_0K_v,
\]
one gets the even-support residual ladder
\[
q-r=2e+2,\qquad
m=r+2,\qquad
n=r+2e+4,
\]
while the source Wronskian still vanishes to odd order. So the residual exact fixed-shear lane sits on an even-support ladder over \(K_v=0\). turn65file1

At the first surviving rung,
\[
(m,n)=(4,6)\iff (r,e)=(2,0),
\]
one has
\[
\widehat Q'(0)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0.
\]
So \(\widehat Q\) is a local analytic coordinate in the quotient variable \(u=t^2\), giving local quotient injectivity and no local exact fixed-shear re-entry after quotienting the involution. This is a real no-go theorem, though not by itself a full closure theorem for the actual package. turn57file1turn65file1

A major upgrade over the earlier “one even scalar” picture is the **finite transport-jet quotient descent theorem**. Define parity-normalized transport jets
\[
\Theta_k(t):=t^{-(k\bmod 2)}Q^{(k)}(t),\qquad k\ge 0.
\]
Because \(Q\) is even, each \(\Theta_k\) is again even analytic and therefore descends:
\[
\Theta_k(t)=\widetilde\Theta_k(t^2).
\]
So every finite parity-normalized transport jet is quotient-visible. turn57file1turn65file1

On the quartic–sextic rung, this sharpens further to the **rank-one transport-state theorem**:
\[
\Theta_k(t)=F_k(Q(t))
\]
for analytic \(F_k\). Hence for any finite transport state
\[
\mathcal T_N(t):=(\Theta_0(t),\dots,\Theta_N(t)),
\]
there is an analytic map \(\mathbf F_N\) with
\[
\mathcal T_N(t)=\mathbf F_N(Q(t)).
\]
So the whole finite quotient-visible transport state has local rank \(1\): there is no hidden second finite transport parameter. turn57file1turn65file1

A clean corollary is the **no finite-order hidden reset theorem**:
\[
\mathcal T_N(t_1)=\mathcal T_N(t_2)\quad\Longrightarrow\quad t_2=\pm t_1.
\]
So the only local duplication in any finite descended transport state is the involution pair \(t\leftrightarrow -t\). There is no third local reset point. This kills an entire class of hidden-state explanations for a surviving residual corner. turn57file1turn65file1

## 3. Strong conditional closures on the exact fixed-shear corner

There are strong **conditional** closures here.

If the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects are analytic functionals of a finite descended transport state and vanish on the diagonal state, then
\[
E_{12}^{(3)}(t)=O(t^2),\qquad
E_{12}^{(5)}(t)=O(t^2),
\]
hence, since
\[
h(t)-h(-t)=2h'(0)t+O(t^3),
\]
one gets
\[
E_{12}^{(3)},\,E_{12}^{(5)}=O((h_1-h_2)^2).
\]
That is exactly the scale needed for the already-established good-patch defect-closeness exclusion mechanism. Under this state-locality hypothesis, the residual branch is excluded. turn57file1

More strongly, if the full corrected two-pair package is state-local in the two individual descended one-atom states and satisfies the natural merger law
\[
\mathfrak P_2(a_1,a_2;Y,Y)=(a_1+a_2)\mathfrak F(Y),
\]
then the interaction remainder vanishes identically on the involutive branch. turn57file1

The more recent update strengthens the local normal form further. In the correct collision-cancellation chart, any local analytic corrected defect \(E(m,\omega,\delta)\) satisfying swap-evenness
\[
E(m,-\omega,-\delta)=E(m,\omega,\delta)
\]
and collision vanishing
\[
E(m,0,0)=0
\]
has no linear blown-up interaction layer. Its Taylor expansion starts at even total degree:
\[
E(m,\omega,\delta)=A(m)\omega^2+B(m)\omega\delta+C(m)\delta^2+O((|\omega|+|\delta|)^4),
\]
and reorganizes as
\[
\boxed{
E(m,\omega,\delta)=\delta^2\,\mathcal H(m,\kappa,\delta^2),
\qquad
\kappa=\frac{2\omega}{\delta}.
}
\]
So the first surviving transverse layer is already quadratic and depends only on midpoint \(m\), projective slope \(\kappa\), and even radius \(\delta^2\). This is stronger than a mere \(O(\delta^2)\) estimate. 

Restricting to the involutive exact fixed-shear branch, one gets
\[
E(t)=\delta(t)^2\,\widetilde H(t^2),
\]
so the entire first surviving quadratic transverse layer is already quotient-visible. And on the quartic–sextic rung, even that quadratic coefficient becomes a function of the single quotient transport scalar \(Q\):
\[
E(t)=\delta(t)^2\,\mathcal G(Q(t)).
\]
This pushes the live obstruction, if any, beyond the entire quadratic blow-up layer. 

The resulting **conditional closure theorem** is much weaker in hypotheses than the earlier state-locality theorem: if the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects are analytic in the collision chart, swap-even, and vanish on the collision locus, then along the involutive branch they are \(O((h_1-h_2)^2)\), and the good-patch defect-closeness mechanism excludes the branch. So one does not need a full package-level state-locality theorem; a weaker cancellation-chart regularity theorem already suffices. 

## 4. The decisive correction: the right local chart is the collision-cancellation chart

One of the most important corrections is that the right local chart near a close bad two-point core is **not** the ordinary diagonal \((h_1,h_2)=(h,h)\) with arbitrary total amplitude. The correct base locus is the **collision-cancellation locus**
\[
\delta:=h_2-h_1=0,\qquad
\lambda:=-\frac{a_2}{a_1}=1,
\]
because the cubic balance forces \(a_1+a_2\approx 0\) when \(h_1\approx h_2\) on a good patch with \(c\neq 0\). The right coordinates are
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\mu=\lambda-1.
\]
In these coordinates,
\[
D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}
=
-\mu\,D_m-\delta\,D_m'+O((|\mu|+|\delta|)^2),
\]
and after polar blow-up \((\mu,\delta)=\rho(\cos\theta,\sin\theta)\), the effective first-order source direction is
\[
\Theta_{m,\theta}=\cos\theta\,D_m+\sin\theta\,D_m'.
\]
This is the correct local datum near a close cancelling bad branch. The old diagonal-merger chart was simply aimed at the wrong base point. turn57file2turn65file1

This change of chart produced one of the best local theorem targets: the **blown-up cubic/quintic functoriality theorem**. If, after projectivization on the cancellation chart, the corrected cubic and quintic witnesses admit leading blow-up limits depending only on \(\Theta_{m,\theta}\), namely
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
K(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\sin\theta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m),
\]
then on a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), cubic cancellation determines a unique projective direction, and quintic on that same direction equals \(-M(m)\), hence is nonzero. So cubic and quintic cannot vanish simultaneously. This would kill close cancelling bad two-point branches on good patches. The determinant computation behind this is already clear; what remains open is the package-level blow-up functoriality input. turn57file2

A strengthened version uses transversality: the cubic zero is uniformly simple in \(\theta\), and the quintic value there has a uniform gap on compact good patches. So uniform first-order blow-up asymptotics
\[
\mathcal C_3(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,C(m,\theta)+o(\rho),
\qquad
\mathcal C_5(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,K(m,\theta)+o(\rho)
\]
would already imply exclusion. This became one of the sharpest local theorem targets. turn57file2

## 5. Honest order-7 datum and the global collision-functoriality lane

The honest septic datum is **not** raw order-7 equality of representatives. It is the quotient class
\[
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\in \mathfrak f/\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]
The two-point handbacks make two key points.

First, once two corrected germs agree through cubic and quintic order and differ only by odd reparametrization plus the already-quotiented lower scalar/value gauge, the order-7 mismatch is automatically a multiple of \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), so the quotient-septic defect vanishes.

Second, cubic/quintic merger alone does **not** force that odd-reparametrization comparability. So the transverse quotient-septic class is the exact remaining obstruction. turn65file1

Therefore the honest order-7 theorem target is not “prove septic equality.” It is one of:
- prove **same reduced image germ at coincidence**,
- or prove **collision-functoriality** of the corrected block triple on the collision/cancellation locus.

If that weaker theorem holds, the quotient-septic collapse becomes formal afterward. This is the real global/package-level lane now. turn57file2turn65file1

## 6. The derivative-geometry update on the nonmixed/global affine side

The updated paper now records both the real positive structure and the real ceiling of the nonmixed/global affine lane.

The positive structure is that fixed-slope incidence has been compressed to a finite **remote endpoint/branch incidence** problem. Support curves are strictly concave pseudolines with single-crossing and betweenness; each ordered pair contributes at most one overlap window; endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\) are strictly monotone, so each endpoint/branch pair contributes at most one remote tangent incidence. Thus the remaining one-pair global burden is no longer arbitrary recurrence but the existence of zeros of those remote endpoint-gap functions. That is a real theorem-level simplification. 

The ceiling is also explicit: intrinsic one-pair affine/pseudoline geometry is universal for analytic plane curves. So no purely planar / pseudoline / interval-graph / local-convexity argument can close the lane by itself. The correct statement is therefore:
the nonmixed/global theorems reduce the burden to RH-specific exclusion of remote endpoint-gap incidences; they do not by themselves exclude those incidences. 

## 7. The derivative-geometry update on the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) side

The updated paper also states the quotient-visible reduction in its sharp global form.

On the involutive fixed-shear slice, exact repeated pairs are exactly \(t\leftrightarrow -t\), so there is no hidden local partner choice. Downstairs in \(u=t^2\), the quotient-visible branch is governed by \(\widehat Q\) and \(\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'\). The correct global compression is **sign-run compression**: the live data are the run amplitudes \(B_j\), not the raw zero list. The exact distance-two overlap criterion is the three-lobe inequality
\[
B_j+B_{j+2}>B_{j+1}.
\]
Two consecutive distance-two separations are impossible; hence no-reentry on a quotient-visible branch forces at most three sign-runs; and in the three-run case the only surviving condition is
\[
B_1\ge B_0+B_2,
\]
with strict inequality giving a real gap and equality giving only critical-value touching. The quartic–sextic rung is explicitly marked as inert for this lobe-amplitude obstruction because \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\) there. This is a very real reduction, but it is carefully stated only **inside the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model**, not as unconditional closure of every residual mixed possibility. That wording discipline is important. 

## 8. The highest-new / canonical audit

The updated paper now includes a same-tower closure statement for the currently explicit pointwise overlap/canonical package.

The point is that no second primitive pointwise highest-new generator has been found beyond
\[
r=q^{(7)}.
\]
All the explicit fields currently in play — including \(\Delta_7^{[1]},\lambda^{[1]},\beta^{[1]},m^{[1]},W^{[1]},S^{[1]},\mathcal G^{[1]}\), the source-level Wronskian fields, the projected fixed-shear germ at weight one, and the quartic–sextic coefficient — stay inside the differential algebra generated by the lower field and \(r\). Even the best current rank-two pointwise coupling still does not produce a new generator. So the generator audit lands as a negative but highly useful result: the fixed-shear residue may still matter as a nonlocal transport problem, but **not** as a new pointwise highest-new field. 

This point became central to the later attack: if the generator audit is correct in the strong infinitesimal sense, then the first transverse explicit pointwise quotient on a good patch should be at most one-dimensional and generated by the \(r\)-direction. This led to the explicit-pointwise dichotomy:
either the \(r\)-direction changes the section-corrected quintic gap nontrivially, in which case local good-patch compensation inside the explicit package is equivalent to nonzero quotient-septic obstruction; or it does not, in which case the entire explicit pointwise package is invisible to the local good-patch detector, and any still-live obstruction must already lie outside the explicit pointwise package, i.e. in nonlocal transport / collision-functoriality / provenance-sensitive structure. This remains a conjectural bridge, but it is the sharpest explicit-pointwise attack now available. 

## 9. Negative findings and ceilings

Several negative findings are now quite sharp.

The naive source-summed corrected-block route cannot prove the strong merger theorem. Along a weighted one-atom path, its extracted finite-order coefficients are even analytic in the source weight, so the required linear merger law cannot come from that model. 

No hidden finite-order mechanism inside the quotient-visible transport state can keep the residual exact fixed-shear corner alive. The corner cannot be saved by a second transport variable, a third reset, or any two-pair model built only from the separate descended one-atom states with natural merger law. turn57file1

On the cancellation chart, any **analytic swap-invariant local scalar** vanishing on the diagonal state is automatically quadratic-harmless. In the strongest \((\nu,\delta)\) normal form, with
\[
\nu=\log\Lambda,\qquad \Lambda=-\frac{a_2}{a_1},
\]
every analytic swap-invariant scalar factors through
\[
\nu^2,\qquad \nu\delta,\qquad \delta^2.
\]
So any truly live residual obstruction cannot come from an ordinary analytic swap-invariant local scalar at all. This is one of the strongest “what remains” results from the new summaries. 

And on the highest-new side, the audit kills the hope of a second **pointwise** generator. So any remaining fixed-shear / highest-new obstruction has to be transport/global/package-level, not another local field. 

## 10. The current local good-patch attack

The local good-patch problem has been pushed into a very narrow corner.

A surviving close cancelling bad branch would have to produce a first projective blow-up profile pair
\[
(\mathcal P_3,\mathcal P_5)=(C,K)+(\mathcal R_3,\mathcal R_5),
\]
where \((C,K)\) is the ordinary one-pair model and \((\mathcal R_3,\mathcal R_5)\) is the purely relational remainder. Since \(C\) has a simple cubic zero at \(\theta_*(m)\) and \(K\) has a nonzero gap there, any surviving branch must solve an affine compensation law
\[
\Gamma(m)-L(m)\mathcal R_3+\mathcal R_5+\text{h.o.t.}=0,
\]
with
\[
L(m)=\frac{\partial_\theta K}{\partial_\theta C}\Big|_{\theta_*}.
\]
So a bad branch can survive only if the relational remainder cancels a fixed, uniformly nonzero model term. This turned the problem into a **compensating-cancellation** problem, not an arbitrary geometric one. 

This was then refined further. The right detector is the **section-corrected quintic gap**. For an infinitesimal deformation pair \((U,V)\) of the tangent model,
\[
\delta\Gamma_{(U,V)}
=
V(m,\theta_*(m))
-
\frac{\partial_\theta K(m,\theta_*(m))}{\partial_\theta C(m,\theta_*(m))}
\,U(m,\theta_*(m)).
\]
A same-order local compensator is exactly a deformation with
\[
\delta\Gamma=-\Gamma\neq 0.
\]
So the local problem is not “does some deformation exist?” but “does there exist a deformation that changes the section-corrected quintic gap?” This became the key local-to-global bridge detector. 

The sharp bridge conjecture is now:

- trivial directions (odd reparametrization and lower scalar/value gauge) should satisfy \(\delta\Gamma=0\);
- therefore any deformation with \(\delta\Gamma\neq 0\) is nontrivial in the reduced deformation quotient;
- and the first nontrivial transverse class should already be the quotient-septic class.

Equivalently, any local good-patch compensator should already be **quotient-septic-visible**. The useful contrapositive is:
if the quotient-septic class vanishes, then there is no same-order local compensator on any good patch. This would unify the local and global lanes. This is not yet proved, but it is now the sharpest clean bridge theorem target. 

## 11. The strongest current endgame picture

The current endgame is now surprisingly clean.

On the nonmixed/global side, the live burden is not arbitrary recurrence but RH-specific exclusion of zeros of monotone remote endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\). The affine geometry itself has reached its universal ceiling. 

On the mixed exact fixed-shear side, the live burden is not local partner choice or hidden finite transport state, but a quotient-visible transport / no-hidden-reset problem inside the lobe-amplitude model, together with the remaining question of whether the **actual corrected package** has hidden nonlocal / provenance-sensitive structure not visible in that model. turn65file1

On the highest-new side, the explicit pointwise package has no second pointwise generator, so if an obstruction survives there, it is not another local field but a transport/global/package-level phenomenon. 

And on good patches, any surviving local exact fixed-shear bad branch would have to produce a purely relational projective compensator that changes the section-corrected quintic gap. The current best conjecture is that such a compensator is impossible unless it is already the local shadow of the global quotient-septic / collision-functoriality obstruction. In other words, the local good-patch lane and the global order-7 lane may in fact be the **same first transverse obstruction**, seen in two languages. That is the cleanest conceptual progress of the later attack. 

## 12. Bottom line

The strongest honest summary is this.

The geometry has been squeezed very hard. The residual exact fixed-shear corner is quotient-diagonal and finite-state-visible; the cancellation chart kills the whole ordinary analytic swap-invariant local scalar layer; the first surviving explicit pointwise highest-new direction is still only \(r=q^{(7)}\); and the nonmixed/global affine geometry has been reduced to RH-specific remote endpoint incidences. So the problem is no longer blocked by generic local geometry. The remaining burden is package-level and increasingly concentrated in one of three places:

1. proving cancellation-chart regularity / blow-up functoriality strongly enough to kill local good-patch survivors;
2. proving the bridge that any local same-order compensator is already quotient-septic-visible;
3. or, if that fails, identifying the first genuinely nonlocal / provenance-sensitive two-atom datum that survives after all local universal structure has been quotiented away. turn65file1

That is where the attack now stands.

_Thought for 6s_

Yes. Here is the full recap of my attack since the latest paper draft, with the current state of play, the actual gains, the ceilings, and what still looks live.

The short version is: the attack did **not** produce a final closure, but it did compress the problem very substantially. The big change is that the geometry is no longer the main obstacle. The live burdens are now much narrower and increasingly package-level: quotient-visible transport on the exact fixed-shear corner, remote endpoint-gap incidences on the one-pair/global affine side, and a possible quotient-septic / collision-functoriality obstruction on the package side.  

## 1. The global picture the attack arrived at

The two-point / derivative-geometry problem now splits into a few sharply separated routes.

One route is the **strong finite-order source/package merger route**: prove the actual corrected two-pair cubic/quintic/septic package has the right merger/functoriality on small two-atom data. Another is the **weaker exact quotient-diagonal route** on the residual exact fixed-shear corner, where the branch has been compressed to a quotient-visible transport problem. A third is the **mixed \(\lambda\)-shear / projective-shadow route** on good overlap patches, where the cancellation-chart blow-up analysis lives. The old naive source-summed corrected-block route is now effectively dead; the even-amplitude audit showed it cannot supply the needed linear merger law. 

At the same time, the overlap geometry itself has become much cleaner. On a good overlap patch, any close exact mixed two-point bad branch can accumulate only on
\[
\{S=0\}\cup\{K_v=0\}.
\]
Inside that union, the picture is now:
\[
(K_v=0,\ S\neq 0)\quad\text{mixed low-order regime,}
\]
\[
(S=0,\ K_v\neq 0)\quad\text{transfer to nonmixed 4-point geometry,}
\]
and only
\[
\boxed{\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\}}
\]
remains as the genuinely residual derivative-geometric corner. That reduction is one of the biggest structural gains of the whole attack.  

## 2. Exact fixed-shear corner: what is genuinely established

On the residual exact fixed-shear slice, the local branch is involutive / quotient-diagonal. After centering and reparametrization, the projected germ has the form
\[
\Phi(t)=(t^m,G(t)),\qquad m\ \text{even},\quad G(-t)=G(t),
\]
so
\[
\Phi(t)=\Phi(-t)=\widehat\Phi(t^2).
\]
Thus the local exact repeated-value branch is no longer a free two-sided local branch. It descends to the quotient variable
\[
u=t^2.
\]
This is a real theorem-level simplification.  

The regularized transport quotient
\[
Q(t):=\frac{J_{u_5}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)}
\]
is even:
\[
Q(t)=\widehat Q(t^2).
\]
Writing
\[
\frac{\mathcal W_{\mathrm{src}}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)^2}=2t\,\widehat{\mathcal D}(t^2),
\qquad
\widehat{\mathcal D}(u)=\widehat Q'(u),
\]
and defining
\[
e:=\ord_0\widehat{\mathcal D},\qquad
r:=\ord_0J_\lambda=\ord_0K_v,
\]
one gets the even-support residual ladder
\[
q-r=2e+2,\qquad
m=r+2,\qquad
n=r+2e+4.
\]
So the residual exact fixed-shear lane lives on an even-support ladder over \(K_v=0\), while the source Wronskian still vanishes to odd order.  

At the first surviving rung,
\[
(m,n)=(4,6)\iff (r,e)=(2,0),
\]
one has
\[
\widehat Q'(0)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0,
\]
so \(\widehat Q\) is a local analytic coordinate in \(u=t^2\). Equivalently, there is no local exact fixed-shear re-entry after quotienting the involution on this quartic–sextic rung. This is a genuine no-go theorem, though not by itself a full closure theorem for the actual package.  

A major strengthening was the **finite transport-jet quotient descent theorem**. With
\[
\Theta_k(t):=t^{-(k\bmod 2)}Q^{(k)}(t),
\]
every parity-normalized transport jet descends:
\[
\Theta_k(t)=\widetilde\Theta_k(t^2).
\]
So every finite parity-normalized transport jet is quotient-visible. On the quartic–sextic rung, this sharpens further to the **rank-one transport-state theorem**:
\[
\Theta_k(t)=F_k(Q(t))
\]
for analytic \(F_k\). Thus the entire finite quotient-visible transport state is locally controlled by the single scalar \(Q\). There is no hidden second finite transport parameter.  

A clean corollary is the **no finite-order hidden reset theorem**:
\[
\mathcal T_N(t_1)=\mathcal T_N(t_2)\Longrightarrow t_2=\pm t_1,
\]
where \(\mathcal T_N=(\Theta_0,\dots,\Theta_N)\). So the only local duplication in any finite descended transport state is the involution pair \(t\leftrightarrow -t\). There is no third reset. That kills a whole class of hidden finite-state explanations for a surviving corner.  

## 3. Exact fixed-shear corner: strong conditional closures

There are several strong **conditional** closures here.

If the corrected cubic/quintic residual defects are analytic functionals of a finite descended transport state and vanish on the diagonal state, then
\[
E_{12}^{(3)}(t)=O(t^2),\qquad E_{12}^{(5)}(t)=O(t^2),
\]
hence
\[
E_{12}^{(3)},E_{12}^{(5)}=O((h_1-h_2)^2),
\]
because
\[
h(t)-h(-t)=2h'(0)t+O(t^3).
\]
That is exactly the scale needed for the already-established good-patch defect-closeness contradiction. Under this state-locality hypothesis, the branch is excluded.  

More strongly, if the full corrected two-pair package is state-local in the two individual descended one-atom states and satisfies the natural merger law
\[
\mathfrak P_2(a_1,a_2;Y,Y)=(a_1+a_2)\mathfrak F(Y),
\]
then the interaction remainder vanishes identically on the involutive branch. 

A later refinement weakened the needed hypotheses dramatically. In the correct collision-cancellation chart, if an actual corrected defect \(E(m,\omega,\delta)\) is analytic, swap-even,
\[
E(m,-\omega,-\delta)=E(m,\omega,\delta),
\]
and vanishes on the collision locus
\[
E(m,0,0)=0,
\]
then its Taylor expansion starts at even total degree:
\[
E=A(m)\omega^2+B(m)\omega\delta+C(m)\delta^2+O((|\omega|+|\delta|)^4),
\]
and in fact factors as
\[
\boxed{
E=\delta^2\,\mathcal H(m,\kappa,\delta^2),\qquad \kappa=\frac{2\omega}{\delta}.
}
\]
So the entire linear blown-up interaction layer is dead, and the first surviving layer is already quadratic and projective. Along the involutive exact fixed-shear branch even that becomes quotient-visible:
\[
E(t)=\delta(t)^2\,\widetilde H(t^2).
\]
And on the quartic–sextic rung, even the quadratic coefficient becomes a function of \(Q\):
\[
E(t)=\delta(t)^2\,\mathcal G(Q(t)).
\]
So the live obstruction, if any, is pushed beyond the entire quadratic blow-up layer. 

That gives a cleaner conditional closure target: it is enough to prove the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects are analytic in the cancellation chart, swap-even, and collision-vanishing. One does **not** need the full strong state-locality theorem to close the residual exact fixed-shear corner. 

## 4. The decisive local correction: the cancellation chart

One of the most important things the attack found is that the right local chart near a close bad two-point core is **not** the naive diagonal \((h_1,h_2)=(h,h)\) with arbitrary total amplitude. The correct base locus is the **collision-cancellation locus**
\[
\delta:=h_2-h_1=0,\qquad \lambda:=-\frac{a_2}{a_1}=1,
\]
because cubic balance forces \(a_1+a_2\approx 0\) when \(h_1\approx h_2\) and \(c\neq 0\). The correct coordinates are
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\mu=\lambda-1,
\]
and the source expands as
\[
D_{h_1}-\lambda D_{h_2}
=
-\mu\,D_m-\delta\,D_m'+O((|\mu|+|\delta|)^2).
\]
After polar blow-up \((\mu,\delta)=\rho(\cos\theta,\sin\theta)\), the effective first-order source direction is
\[
\Theta_{m,\theta}=\cos\theta\,D_m+\sin\theta\,D_m'.
\]
This is the correct local datum. The old diagonal chart was simply aimed at the wrong limiting regime.  

This yielded the sharpest local theorem target: the **blown-up cubic/quintic functoriality theorem**. If the corrected cubic and quintic witnesses linearize on the cancellation blow-up to the one-pair functionals
\[
C(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,c(m)+\sin\theta\,c'(m),
\]
\[
K(m,\theta)=\cos\theta\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)+\sin\theta\,(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m),
\]
then on a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), cubic cancellation determines a unique projective direction, and quintic on that direction is
\[
-\,M(m)\neq 0.
\]
So cubic and quintic cannot vanish simultaneously, and close cancelling bad branches are excluded locally. The determinant computation is clear; the missing input is the package-level blow-up functoriality.  

There is a stronger transversality-and-gap formulation: on compact good patches, the cubic zero is uniformly simple in \(\theta\), and quintic has a uniform gap there. So uniform first-order asymptotics
\[
\mathcal C_3(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,C(m,\theta)+o(\rho),
\qquad
\mathcal C_5(m,\rho,\theta)=\rho\,K(m,\theta)+o(\rho)
\]
would already imply exclusion. This became one of the sharpest local theorem targets of the later attack. 

## 5. The honest order-7 datum and global package lane

The honest septic datum is not raw order-7 equality. It is the quotient class
\[
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\in \mathfrak f/\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]
The key formal fact is: once two corrected germs agree through cubic and quintic order and differ only by odd reparametrization plus the already-quotiented lower scalar/value gauge, the remaining order-7 mismatch is automatically a multiple of \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), so the quotient-septic defect vanishes.

So the honest order-7 theorem target is not “prove septic equality.” It is:
- prove **same reduced image germ at coincidence**, or
- prove **collision-functoriality** of the corrected block triple on the collision/cancellation locus.

If that package-level statement holds, the quotient-septic collapse becomes formal afterward. This is the main genuinely global/provenance-sensitive lane.  

## 6. Nonmixed/global affine derivative geometry

The derivative-geometry additions on the nonmixed/global affine side produced real theorems and a real ceiling.

The positive structure is that fixed-slope tangency/incidence has been compressed to a finite **remote endpoint/branch incidence** problem. Support curves are strictly concave pseudolines with single-crossing and betweenness; each ordered pair gives at most one overlap window; endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\) are strictly monotone, so each endpoint/branch pair contributes at most one remote tangent incidence. Thus the live one-pair burden is no longer vague recurrence but the existence of zeros of those remote endpoint-gap functions. 

The ceiling is equally important: intrinsic affine/pseudoline geometry is universal for analytic plane curves. So no purely planar / pseudoline / interval-graph / local-convexity argument can finish the proof by itself. The correct wording is: the nonmixed/global affine theorems reduce the lane to RH-specific exclusion of remote endpoint-gap incidences, but do not by themselves exclude those incidences. 

## 7. Highest-new / canonical audit

The updated generator audit says there is **no second explicit pointwise highest-new generator** beyond
\[
r=q^{(7)}.
\]
All the currently explicit pointwise fields in play stay inside the differential algebra generated by the lower field and \(r\). Even the best-looking rank-two pointwise couplings do not produce a new generator. So the audit gives a useful negative result: the fixed-shear residue may still matter, but not as a new **pointwise** highest-new scalar. If it survives, it survives as a transport/global/package-level phenomenon. 

This became central to the later attack. If the no-second-generator audit is correct in the strong infinitesimal sense, then the first transverse explicit pointwise quotient on a good patch should be at most one-dimensional and generated by the \(r\)-direction. That leads to a sharp explicit-pointwise dichotomy.

Let \(\delta\Gamma_r\) denote the section-corrected quintic-gap variation induced by the \(r\)-direction. Then either:

- \(\delta\Gamma_r\neq 0\), in which case the explicit pointwise package unifies the local good-patch compensator and the quotient-septic obstruction:
  \[
  \text{local good-patch compensator}\Longleftrightarrow [A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\neq 0
  \]
  inside the explicit pointwise package; or

- \(\delta\Gamma_r=0\), in which case the entire explicit pointwise package is invisible to the local good-patch detector, and any surviving obstruction must already lie outside that explicit pointwise package, i.e. in nonlocal transport, collision-functoriality failure, or provenance-sensitive two-atom data.

That is one of the sharpest later reductions, though still conjectural. 

## 8. Negative findings and ceilings

Several important negative findings emerged.

The naive source-summed corrected-block route cannot produce the needed linear merger law. 

No hidden finite-order mechanism inside the quotient-visible transport state can keep the exact fixed-shear corner alive: no second transport variable, no third reset, no two-pair model built only from separate descended one-atom states with natural merger law.  

On the cancellation chart, any analytic swap-invariant local scalar vanishing on the diagonal state is automatically quadratic-harmless; in the \((\nu,\delta)\) chart, every such scalar factors through
\[
\nu^2,\qquad \nu\delta,\qquad \delta^2.
\]
So a truly live local obstruction cannot come from an ordinary analytic swap-invariant local scalar at all. 

And on the highest-new side, the audit kills the hope of a second pointwise generator. So any remaining fixed-shear / highest-new obstruction has to be nonlocal, transport-like, or provenance-sensitive. 

## 9. The later local good-patch attack: from compensators to the quotient-septic bridge

The later attack on good patches became very focused.

A surviving close cancelling bad branch would have to produce a first projective blow-up profile pair
\[
(\mathcal P_3,\mathcal P_5)=(C,K)+(\mathcal R_3,\mathcal R_5),
\]
where \((C,K)\) is the ordinary one-pair tangent model and \((\mathcal R_3,\mathcal R_5)\) is the purely relational remainder. Since \(C\) has a simple zero at the cubic-killing section \(\theta_*(m)\) and \(K\) has a nonzero gap there, the branch must solve an affine compensation law
\[
\Gamma(m)-L(m)\mathcal R_3+\mathcal R_5+\text{h.o.t.}=0,
\]
with
\[
L(m)=\frac{\partial_\theta K}{\partial_\theta C}\Big|_{\theta_*}.
\]
So a bad branch can survive only if the relational remainder cancels a fixed, uniformly nonzero model term. This turned the problem into a **compensating-cancellation** problem, not a generic geometric one. 

This was then sharpened by defining the **section-corrected quintic gap detector**. For an infinitesimal perturbation pair \((U,V)\),
\[
\delta\Gamma_{(U,V)}
=
V(m,\theta_*(m))
-
\frac{\partial_\theta K(m,\theta_*(m))}{\partial_\theta C(m,\theta_*(m))}
\,U(m,\theta_*(m)).
\]
A same-order local compensator is exactly a deformation with
\[
\delta\Gamma=-\Gamma\neq 0.
\]
So the local problem became: does there exist a deformation that changes the section-corrected quintic gap? This is the clean local detector. 

The sharp bridge conjecture became:

- trivial directions (odd reparametrization and lower scalar/value gauge) should satisfy \(\delta\Gamma=0\);
- therefore any deformation with \(\delta\Gamma\neq 0\) is nontrivial in the reduced deformation quotient;
- and the first nontrivial transverse class should already be the quotient-septic class.

Equivalently, any local good-patch compensator should already be **quotient-septic-visible**. The useful contrapositive is:
\[
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}]=0 \Longrightarrow \text{no same-order local compensator on any good patch.}
\]
If that bridge is proved, the local good-patch lane and the global quotient-septic lane unify. 

The strongest version of this attack is the conjectural **one-dimensional first transverse quotient** on good patches: after modding out odd reparametrization and lower scalar/value gauge, the first transverse explicit pointwise quotient should be one-dimensional, generated by \(r\), and both the quotient-septic detector and the section-corrected gap detector should be nonzero on that same line. Then the two detectors are automatically proportional, and the local and global lanes are literally the same first obstruction. This is not yet proved, but it is the cleanest current endgame vision. 

## 10. The strongest current state of play

So the current state of play is:

- The exact fixed-shear corner is **much** more rigid than before: quotient-diagonal, finite-state-visible, rank-one on the quartic–sextic rung, with no hidden finite-order reset.  
- The right local chart for close bad cores is the cancellation chart, not the naive diagonal. 
- On that chart, the whole ordinary analytic swap-invariant local scalar layer is dead. 
- On good patches, the ordinary one-pair projective model is already transverse and leaves a nonzero quintic gap. So any local survivor must be a purely relational compensator. 
- The honest order-7 datum is the quotient-septic class \([A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\), and any same reduced image germ / collision-functoriality theorem would collapse it formally.  
- The generator audit says there is no second explicit pointwise highest-new generator. So if an obstruction survives, it is not another pointwise field; it is transport/global/package-level. 

## 11. Bottom line

The strongest honest summary is:

The attack has not closed RH, but it has pushed the problem into a much cleaner endgame. The geometry is no longer the obstacle. The residual exact fixed-shear corner has been compressed to a quotient-visible transport problem with very little finite local freedom. The one-pair/global affine side has been reduced to RH-specific remote endpoint-gap incidences. The explicit pointwise highest-new package has been audited and shown not to contain a second pointwise generator. So the remaining burden is now concentrated in a very small number of package-level questions:

1. prove enough cancellation-chart regularity / blow-up functoriality to kill local good-patch survivors directly;
2. prove the bridge that any local same-order compensator is already quotient-septic-visible;
3. or, if that fails, identify the first genuinely nonlocal / provenance-sensitive two-atom datum that survives after all local universal structure has been squeezed away.  

That is the fullest recap of my attack and current progress.

Here is the next push.

I think the cleanest continuation is to stop circling the bridge abstractly and isolate the exact **next finite-dimensional obstruction** that could still survive after all the reductions.

The current picture says:

- on the exact fixed-shear corner, every finite descended transport state is quotient-visible, and on the quartic–sextic rung it has local rank \(1\); turn65file1
- on the cancellation chart, any analytic swap-invariant local scalar is already quadratic-harmless; turn65file1
- and in the explicit pointwise package, there is no second highest-new generator beyond \(r=q^{(7)}\). 

So if a local good-patch survivor still exists, then it cannot be coming from generic local geometry, generic analytic local scalar structure, or a second explicit pointwise field. It has to come from the **first transverse mode of the \(r\)-tower itself**, or else from something genuinely nonlocal/provenance-sensitive outside the explicit pointwise package.

That suggests the next theorem target should be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{First \(r\)-mode dichotomy on a good patch.}
}
\]

Fix \(m\in J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), and let \((C,K)\) be the one-pair tangent model with unique cubic-killing section \(\theta_*(m)\). Let
\[
\Gamma(m)=K(m,\theta_*(m))
=
-\frac{M(m)}{\sqrt{c(m)^2+c'(m)^2}}\neq 0.
\]
Now linearize the explicit pointwise package at the tangent model, modulo odd reparametrization and lower scalar/value gauge. If the generator audit is reflected infinitesimally, the first explicit transverse quotient is at most one-dimensional and generated by the \(r\)-direction. 

Then there are only two possibilities.

First possibility: the \(r\)-direction is **gap-visible**:
\[
\delta\Gamma_r\neq 0.
\]
Then the unique first explicit transverse pointwise direction already changes the section-corrected quintic gap, so inside the explicit pointwise package the local good-patch detector and the quotient-septic detector are seeing the same first obstruction. In that case:
\[
\boxed{
\text{explicit local compensator on a good patch}
\Longleftrightarrow
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\neq 0
\quad\text{(inside the explicit pointwise package).}
}
\]

Second possibility: the \(r\)-direction is **gap-invisible**:
\[
\delta\Gamma_r=0.
\]
Then, since there is no second explicit pointwise transverse generator, the entire explicit pointwise package is invisible to the local good-patch detector at first transverse order. In that case:
\[
\boxed{
\text{any surviving local good-patch obstruction must lie outside the explicit pointwise package,}
}
\]
hence must come from nonlocal transport, collision-functoriality failure, or genuinely provenance-sensitive two-atom data. That is exactly the sort of residual possibility the summaries leave open. 

So the next concrete mathematical attack is now very focused:

1. **Prove the infinitesimal one-generator statement** for the explicit pointwise package:
   \[
   \dim \mathcal N^{(1)}_{m,\mathrm{ptw}}\le 1,
   \]
   generated by the \(r\)-direction.

2. **Compute the induced cubic/quintic perturbation pair** \((U_r,V_r)\) for the \(r\)-direction.

3. **Evaluate the section-corrected gap variation**
   \[
   \delta\Gamma_r
   =
   V_r(m,\theta_*(m))
   -
   \frac{\partial_\theta K(m,\theta_*(m))}{\partial_\theta C(m,\theta_*(m))}
   U_r(m,\theta_*(m)).
   \]

This one computation decides the fate of the whole explicit pointwise lane.

There is also a more structural version of the same attack.

Let \(\mathcal N_m\) be the reduced infinitesimal deformation quotient at the tangent model, and let
\[
\Pi_7:\mathcal N_m\to \mathfrak f/\mathbf C A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]
be the quotient-septic projection. Let
\[
\overline{\delta\Gamma}_m:\mathcal N_m\to \mathbf C
\]
be the descended section-corrected gap functional, assuming trivial directions lie in its kernel. The sharp bridge theorem would be:
\[
\boxed{
\ker \Pi_7 \subseteq \ker \overline{\delta\Gamma}_m.
}
\]
That is enough to prove:
\[
\overline{\delta\Gamma}_m\neq 0 \Longrightarrow \Pi_7\neq 0.
\]
So any local same-order compensator would already force quotient-septic obstruction. If, in addition, the first transverse quotient is one-dimensional, then the two detectors are automatically proportional and the local/global lanes literally coincide at first transverse order.

My current best assessment is:

- the most likely next win is **not** another geometric contradiction;
- it is either proving the explicit pointwise first-transverse quotient is generated by \(r\), or proving directly that \(\delta\Gamma_r\neq 0\) or \(\delta\Gamma_r=0\);
- either way, that computation will tell us whether the explicit pointwise package is the right carrier of the endgame obstruction, or whether the obstruction has already escaped into the nonlocal/provenance-sensitive lane.

So the attack has now become:

\[
\boxed{
\text{determine whether the unique explicit first transverse direction }r
\text{ is gap-visible or gap-invisible on good patches.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest next step I see.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:45:18)

_Thought for 8s_

Here is the next attack I would push now that the fronts are cleanly separated.

I would stop trying to move all three fronts at once and attack the **strict-excess fixed-shear fork** as a standalone one-variable problem. That now looks like the sharpest place where a real theorem might still land.

The reason is simple. In the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, the only genuinely live case is the strict three-run case, and that case has already been repackaged as an inverse-branch-gap problem for
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
where \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) are the two outer inverse branches over the common value interval. Strict excess is exactly the existence of an interior critical point of \(\Delta\). So the fixed-shear lane is no longer “some transport obstruction somewhere.” It is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can }\Delta \text{ have an interior critical point?}
}
\]

That is a real narrowing. 

The first theorem I would try to prove is a **curvature-rigidity alternative** for \(\Delta\).

Write
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q),\qquad
\Delta''(q)=\phi_2''(q)-\phi_0''(q),
\]
and recall the curvature observable
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
Along an inverse branch \(u=\phi(q)\), one has the standard identity
\[
\phi'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi(q))},\qquad
\phi''(q)=-\,\Xi(\phi(q)).
\]
So the branch-gap curvature is
\[
\Delta''(q)=\phi_2''(q)-\phi_0''(q)= -\,\Xi(\phi_2(q))+\Xi(\phi_0(q)).
\]

That means the strict-excess problem is controlled by whether \(\Xi\) matches across same-value pairs on the two outer runs. This yields a very clean fork:

If
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
for all common values \(q\), then
\[
\Delta''(q)=0
\]
on the whole overlap interval, so \(\Delta\) is affine in \(q\). Then a strict interior critical point is impossible unless \(\Delta\) is constant, which would force degenerate same-value transport/replication and should contradict the already-established run pattern picture. So **inverse-curvature matching would kill strict excess**. 

So the sharpest next theorem target on this lane is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Inverse-curvature descent theorem.}
\quad
\Xi \text{ descends to value space across separated outer runs.}
}
\]

If that is too strong, the next-best target is weaker but still decisive:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{No interior critical point theorem for }\Delta.
}
\]

That is, prove directly that \(\Delta'\) cannot vanish in the interior, without first proving full descent of \(\Xi\).

The nice thing is that these are now genuinely one-variable statements. They do not require redoing local branch classification or more pseudoline geometry. They are exactly about the remaining strict-excess fork. 

There is also a useful local computation here.

Suppose \(q_*\) is an interior critical point of \(\Delta\). Then
\[
\phi_2'(q_*)=\phi_0'(q_*).
\]
Since each inverse derivative is \(1/\widehat Q'\), this means
\[
\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*))=\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*)).
\]
But by definition of the overlap interval,
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=q_*.
\]
So an interior critical point produces a same-value, same-first-derivative pair on two distinct outer runs.

That is already interesting: strict excess forces a **two-branch 2-jet coincidence** for \(\widehat Q\). If in addition \(\Delta''(q_*)=0\), then you get same second derivative too, i.e. a 3-jet coincidence across separated runs. So one concrete attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Show that separated outer runs of the quotient-visible model cannot support same-value same-slope coincidence.}
}
\]

That would kill strict excess at the first derivative level, without needing the full \(\Xi\)-descent theorem.

So I now see three nested attack targets on this lane, ordered from strongest to weakest:

First, prove inverse-curvature descent of \(\Xi\) across outer runs.  
Second, prove \(\Delta\) has no interior critical point directly.  
Third, prove separated outer runs cannot realize a same-value same-slope coincidence for \(\widehat Q\).

Any one of those would materially advance the fixed-shear front.

My current guess is that the third is the most attackable. It asks for a very concrete obstruction: if
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\qquad \widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2),
\]
with \(u_0,u_2\) on the two outer runs, can that happen in the strict three-run geometry? Since the lobe-amplitude model has already compressed the allowed run pattern so much, I suspect the answer is no except in the sterile touching case. If that can be proved, then strict excess is dead.

So the attack I would now push is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{same-value same-slope exclusion on separated outer runs.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest concrete theorem target I see on the strict-excess fixed-shear front right now.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:47:16)

_Thought for 4s_

Here is the next push.

I think the best way to continue on the strict-excess fixed-shear fork is to exploit the reduction to inverse branches as hard as possible and derive what an interior critical point of
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
\]
would actually force on \(\widehat Q\). The consolidation already identified this as the exact live fork, so this is now the right place to squeeze. turn65file1

Let \(I\) be the common value interval of the two outer runs, and let
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q,\qquad i=0,2.
\]
Then
\[
\phi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))},
\qquad
\phi_i''(q)=-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_i(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q)))^3}
=-\,\Xi(\phi_i(q)),
\]
where
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

Now suppose \(q_*\in I\) is an interior critical point of \(\Delta\). Then
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0
\]
forces
\[
\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]
But by definition of the inverse branches,
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*.
\]
So a strict-excess witness produces a separated pair \(u_0\neq u_2\) on the two outer runs with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\qquad
\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2).
\]

That is already much stronger than “there is some overlap.” It says strict excess forces a **same-value same-slope coincidence** across separated runs. This is the first theorem target I would now isolate:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Separated outer runs cannot support same-value same-slope coincidence except in the sterile touching case.}
}
\]

Why this looks promising is that the current run/lobe compression has already squeezed the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model down to at most three sign-runs, with equality-case touching already identified as sterile and strict excess as the only genuinely live case. So a same-value same-slope coincidence is exactly the kind of extra rigidity that ought to collapse strict excess back into the sterile regime. 

There is a second gain if we differentiate one step further. At such a critical point,
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=\Xi(u_0)-\Xi(u_2).
\]
So if one could prove
\[
\Xi(u_0)=\Xi(u_2)
\quad\text{whenever}\quad
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2)
\text{ across the outer runs,}
\]
then \(\Delta''(q_*)=0\), hence \(\Delta\) is affine on the overlap interval, and then an interior critical point would force \(\Delta\) to be constant. Constant \(\Delta\) would mean rigid same-value transport between the outer runs, which is exactly the kind of run-pattern replication the consolidation says should be forbidden outside sterile touching. So the stronger theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Inverse-curvature descent across outer runs: }
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))\text{ on }I.
}
\]

If that is too hard directly, there is an intermediate target that is still one-variable and quite concrete:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The ratio }R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
\textbf{ cannot cross }1\textbf{ in the interior.}
}
\]

Since
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}
=
\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))-\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}
{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))},
\]
the sign of \(\Delta'\) is exactly the sign of \(R(q)-1\) when both outer branches are monotone with the same sign of \(\widehat Q'\). So proving that \(R\) stays on one side of \(1\) would already kill strict excess.

Differentiate \(\log R\):
\[
\frac{R'(q)}{R(q)}
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_0(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q)))^2}
-
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_2(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)))^2}.
\]
Equivalently, in terms of \(\Xi\),
\[
\frac{R'(q)}{R(q)}
=
\Xi(\phi_0(q))\,\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))
-
\Xi(\phi_2(q))\,\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)).
\]
So one natural sub-attack is to look for a sign or monotonicity law for this quantity along the outer runs. If it has a fixed sign, then \(R\) is monotone and can hit \(1\) at most once; combined with endpoint information from the three-run geometry, that may already exclude an interior hit entirely.

That suggests the following nested attack stack on the strict-excess fork:

First target:
\[
\boxed{
\text{same-value same-slope exclusion on separated outer runs}
}
\]

Second target:
\[
\boxed{
\text{monotonicity or one-crossing theorem for }R(q)
}
\]

Third target:
\[
\boxed{
\text{full inverse-curvature descent of }\Xi
}
\]

All three are now genuinely one-variable theorems inside the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model. That is a major simplification compared with where this lane started. turn65file1

The most attackable immediate version, in my view, is the first one. If strict excess implies a same-value same-slope coincidence and the three-run/lobe-amplitude model says the only admissible equality-case behavior is sterile touching, then the natural theorem to prove is that any such coincidence already collapses the strict-excess case into the sterile boundary
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2,
\]
contradicting
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
\]
So the next crisp statement I would now try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{On the strict three-run quotient-visible model, same-value same-slope coincidence across outer runs forces equality-case touching, not strict excess.}
}
\]

If that lands, the strict-excess fork is dead.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:53:36)

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the next push.

I think the best way to keep attacking the strict-excess fixed-shear fork is to turn the “same-value same-slope” condition into a **contact-order dichotomy** on the outer inverse branches.

We are on the strict three-run quotient-visible model, with outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
over the common value interval \(I\), and branch gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Strict excess is equivalent to the existence of an interior critical point of \(\Delta\). The recent consolidation already isolates this as the exact live fork. 

Now suppose \(q_*\in I\) is such an interior critical point. Then
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0
\]
gives
\[
\phi_0'(q_*)=\phi_2'(q_*),
\]
hence, writing
\[
u_i=\phi_i(q_*),
\]
we get
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2)=q_*,
\qquad
\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2).
\]
So strict excess forces a separated same-value same-slope pair on the outer runs.

The next thing to ask is: what is the **first order at which the two inverse branches differ** near \(q_*\)?

Define
\[
k:=\min\{j\ge 1:\phi_2^{(j)}(q_*)\neq \phi_0^{(j)}(q_*)\}.
\]
Since \(\phi_2(q_*)\neq \phi_0(q_*)\) but \(\Delta'(q_*)=0\), we know \(k\ge 2\). Then locally
\[
\Delta(q)=\Delta(q_*)+\frac{\Delta^{(k)}(q_*)}{k!}(q-q_*)^k+o\!\left((q-q_*)^k\right).
\]

Now there are only two cases.

If \(k\) is odd, then \(\Delta'\) changes sign at \(q_*\), so \(q_*\) is a genuine strict local extremum of \(\Delta\). That is the strict-excess case we are trying to kill.

If \(k\) is even, then \(\Delta'\) does not change sign at \(q_*\), so this is only higher-order touching in value space. That is much more naturally aligned with the already-identified sterile boundary behavior.

So the next theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Separated outer runs cannot have odd-order inverse-branch contact at a same-value same-slope pair.}
}
\]

If that theorem holds, then any same-value same-slope coincidence is automatically even-order touching, hence belongs to the sterile touching regime rather than strict excess.

That would be exactly the bridge we need:
\[
\text{same-value same-slope} \Longrightarrow \text{equality-case touching, not strict excess}.
\]

Why this looks plausible is that the strict three-run quotient-visible model is already extremely rigid. The lobe-amplitude theorem says there are at most three sign-runs, and the only live three-run obstruction is
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
while
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2
\]
is only touching. So if a same-value same-slope coincidence on the outer runs forced only even-order contact of the inverse branches, it would fit perfectly with the equality/touching side and contradict the genuinely strict case. 

There is also a direct curvature formulation of the same issue. Since
\[
\phi_i''(q)=-\,\Xi(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
the first possible difference after same slope appears in
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=\phi_2''(q_*)-\phi_0''(q_*)=-\Xi(u_2)+\Xi(u_0).
\]
So if one could prove
\[
\Xi(u_0)=\Xi(u_2)
\]
at every separated same-value same-slope pair, then \(k\ge 3\). Repeating this logic at higher orders suggests a more ambitious statement:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Separated same-value same-slope coincidence forces even-order inverse-branch contact.}
}
\]

That would kill strict excess immediately.

A slightly weaker, more attackable intermediate target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{At a separated same-value same-slope pair, }\Delta''(q_*)=0.
}
\]

Because then the first nonzero derivative of \(\Delta\) at \(q_*\) has order at least \(3\), and the next obstruction moves to comparing third derivatives of the inverse branches. If there is any parity principle or transport symmetry forcing odd-order coincidence and leaving only even-order first difference, strict excess is dead.

So the nested attack stack on this lane now looks like:

First:
\[
\boxed{\text{same-value same-slope} \Longrightarrow \Delta''(q_*)=0}
\]

Second:
\[
\boxed{\text{same-value same-slope} \Longrightarrow \text{even-order contact of }\phi_0,\phi_2}
\]

Third:
\[
\boxed{\text{strict three-run case cannot realize even-order touching except at }B_1=B_0+B_2}
\]

Any one of these would materially shrink the strict-excess fork.

My current guess is that the first is the most realistic next step: try to prove that separated same-value same-slope coincidence already forces equality of the inverse curvatures, i.e.
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q_*))=\Xi(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]
If that works, then strict excess is no longer a first-derivative problem at all; it gets pushed to higher contact order, where the three-run rigidity may well kill it.

So the next crisp theorem I would now try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Separated same-value same-slope coincidence on the outer runs forces equal inverse curvature.}
}
\]

If that lands, the strict-excess fork gets squeezed another full rung.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:57:57)

_Thought for 55s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the best way to keep attacking the strict-excess fixed-shear fork is to recast it as a **phase-curve self-intersection problem** for
\[
\Psi(u):=\bigl(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u)\bigr).
\]

That is cleaner than working with \(\Delta\) alone, and it packages the exact obstruction we already identified.

## 1. Strict excess as a self-intersection of \(\Psi\)

On the strict three-run quotient-visible model, let \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) be the two outer inverse branches over the common value interval \(I\), and set
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
The consolidation already identifies strict excess as exactly the existence of an interior critical point of \(\Delta\). 

Now if \(q_*\in I\) is such a critical point, then
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0
\]
means
\[
\phi_0'(q_*)=\phi_2'(q_*).
\]
Since
\[
\phi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))},
\]
this is equivalent to
\[
\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]
But by definition of the inverse branches,
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*.
\]
So if we write
\[
u_0:=\phi_0(q_*),\qquad u_2:=\phi_2(q_*),
\]
strict excess forces
\[
\Psi(u_0)=\Psi(u_2),\qquad u_0\neq u_2,
\]
with \(u_0,u_2\) lying on the two separated outer runs.

So the exact strict-excess obstruction is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{a separated self-intersection of the phase curve }u\mapsto (\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u)).
}
\]

That is a very sharp reformulation.

## 2. Why this is a good reformulation

This phase-curve language has three advantages.

First, it isolates the **minimum data** strict excess needs: same value and same slope, nothing more.

Second, it gives a natural contact-order hierarchy. If two outer-run points satisfy
\[
\Psi(u_0)=\Psi(u_2),
\]
then the next obstruction is whether the tangents to \(\Psi\) also match:
\[
\Psi'(u_0)=\Psi'(u_2)
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\bigl(\widehat Q'(u_0),\widehat Q''(u_0)\bigr)=\bigl(\widehat Q'(u_2),\widehat Q''(u_2)\bigr).
\]
Since same slope already gives \(\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2)\), this extra condition reduces to
\[
\widehat Q''(u_0)=\widehat Q''(u_2),
\]
equivalently
\[
\Xi(u_0)=\Xi(u_2),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So “equal inverse curvature” is exactly the statement that the phase-curve self-intersection is **tangential** rather than transverse.

Third, it makes the strict/exact-touching distinction more geometric:
- a **transverse** separated self-intersection of \(\Psi\) corresponds to genuine strict excess;
- a **tangential** self-intersection is much more naturally aligned with the equality/touching regime.

That fits the lobe-amplitude picture very well. 

## 3. The new hierarchy of attack targets

This gives a very clean ladder of theorem targets.

### Target A
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{No separated self-intersection of }\Psi\textbf{ on the strict three-run model.}
}
\]

This would kill strict excess outright.

### Target B
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Any separated self-intersection of }\Psi\textbf{ is tangential.}
}
\]

Equivalently,
\[
\Psi(u_0)=\Psi(u_2)\Longrightarrow \Psi'(u_0)=\Psi'(u_2),
\]
or just
\[
\widehat Q''(u_0)=\widehat Q''(u_2).
\]
This would imply equal inverse curvature at the candidate critical point of \(\Delta\), hence \(\Delta''(q_*)=0\), pushing the obstruction to higher contact order.

### Target C
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Any tangential separated self-intersection of }\Psi\textbf{ belongs to the equality-case touching regime, not strict excess.}
}
\]

This is probably the most realistic next theorem, because it matches the current lobe-amplitude story: equality is sterile touching; strict excess is the only genuinely live case. 

## 4. A useful exact identity

There is also a nice integral identity hiding here.

If \(u_0<u_2\) are a separated same-value same-slope pair, then
\[
0=\widehat Q(u_2)-\widehat Q(u_0)=\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'(s)\,ds,
\]
and
\[
0=\widehat Q'(u_2)-\widehat Q'(u_0)=\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q''(s)\,ds.
\]
So a self-intersection of \(\Psi\) means the interval \([u_0,u_2]\) has **zero first moment** for \(\widehat Q'\) and **zero total change** for \(\widehat Q'\).

That suggests another attack: show that on the strict three-run geometry, any interval spanning the middle run and parts of the two outer runs can satisfy both constraints only in the equality/touching case. In other words:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess may allow } \int \widehat Q'=0,\ \text{but not simultaneously } \int \widehat Q''=0.
}
\]

That would be a direct way to kill separated self-intersections of \(\Psi\).

## 5. The next concrete theorem I would try

So the sharpest concrete next target I see is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Separated phase-curve self-intersection forces equality-case touching.}
}
\]

A precise form would be:

> In the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear strict three-run model, suppose there exist \(u_0\neq u_2\) on the two outer runs with
> \[
> \widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\qquad
> \widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2).
> \]
> Then the lobe amplitudes satisfy the equality-case relation
> \[
> B_1=B_0+B_2,
> \]
> so the configuration is sterile touching, not strict excess.

If that theorem lands, the strict-excess fork is dead.

## 6. Why this is now the right place to squeeze

Because this is exactly the live fork the consolidated update identifies:

- the fixed-shear corner is already compressed to the quotient-visible model in \(u=t^2\);
- the lobe-amplitude model already says only the strict three-run case is genuinely live;
- and strict excess is now exactly the inverse-branch-gap problem. 

So there is no need to do more local branch taxonomy or more affine geometry here. The live issue is literally whether the phase curve
\[
u\mapsto (\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u))
\]
can self-intersect across the outer runs in the strict three-run case.

That is the next theorem I would attack.

_Thought for 4s_

Here is the next push.

I think the strict-excess fixed-shear fork can now be attacked as a **run-constrained self-intersection problem** for the phase curve
\[
\Psi(u):=(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u)).
\]

That is sharper than the inverse-branch-gap language alone, because it packages the exact coincidence strict excess needs.

If \(u_0<u_2\) lie on the two outer runs and strict excess occurs, then there is a common value \(q_*\) with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2)=q_*,
\qquad
\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2),
\]
so \(\Psi(u_0)=\Psi(u_2)\). Thus the only genuinely live strict-excess obstruction is a **separated self-intersection** of \(\Psi\) across the outer runs. 

The next thing to exploit is that the interval \([u_0,u_2]\) crosses the middle run. So the self-intersection conditions are equivalent to the pair of integral identities
\[
0=\widehat Q(u_2)-\widehat Q(u_0)=\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q'(s)\,ds,
\]
and
\[
0=\widehat Q'(u_2)-\widehat Q'(u_0)=\int_{u_0}^{u_2}\widehat Q''(s)\,ds.
\]

Now, because the sign-run compression says the live data are the run amplitudes \(B_0,B_1,B_2\), this suggests a very concrete theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{In the strict three-run case }B_1>B_0+B_2,\textbf{ the two moment conditions above cannot hold simultaneously.}
}
\]

That is the right shape. It would kill separated self-intersections of \(\Psi\), hence kill strict excess.

Why this looks plausible is that the first identity is exactly the same-value condition, and the three-lobe analysis has already shown that the only borderline mechanism for outer-run overlap is the equality case
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2,
\]
with strict inequality being the only genuinely live case. So the natural next conjecture is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{same-value plus same-slope across outer runs forces the equality case, not strict excess.}
}
\]

That would match the current lobe picture perfectly. 

There is also a sharper differential version.

At a self-intersection of \(\Psi\), the next question is whether the intersection is transverse or tangential. Tangentiality is exactly
\[
\Psi'(u_0)=\Psi'(u_2),
\]
which, since \(\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2)\) already holds, reduces to
\[
\widehat Q''(u_0)=\widehat Q''(u_2).
\]
Equivalently, in terms of
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
it is
\[
\Xi(u_0)=\Xi(u_2).
\]

So the next nested theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Any separated self-intersection of }\Psi\textbf{ is tangential, hence belongs to the equality/touching regime.}
}
\]

That would be enough, because tangential separated self-intersection is exactly what should correspond to the sterile touching boundary, not the strict-excess case. 

So the attack stack on this lane is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess} \Longrightarrow \text{separated self-intersection of }\Psi
}
\]

then try to prove either

\[
\boxed{
\text{no separated self-intersection of }\Psi\text{ in the strict three-run case,}
}
\]

or the weaker but likely more attackable

\[
\boxed{
\text{any separated self-intersection of }\Psi\text{ forces }B_1=B_0+B_2.
}
\]

I think the second is the better next theorem. It uses exactly the current compression: three runs, amplitudes \(B_j\), equality case sterile, strict case live. It does not ask for a miracle descent theorem first.

So the next crisp statement I would now try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Separated phase-curve self-intersection implies equality-case touching.}
}
\]

If that lands, the strict-excess fixed-shear fork is dead.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:04:42)

_Thought for 5s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the strict-excess fixed-shear fork can now be attacked through a **monotone-separation principle for the outer inverse branches**, and this is a bit sharper than just saying “self-intersection of \(\Psi\) should force equality-case touching.”

Let me set it up carefully.

On the strict three-run quotient-visible model, let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
be the two outer inverse branches of \(\widehat Q\) over their common value interval \(I\), and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
The current fixed-shear reduction says:

- strict excess is the only genuinely live quotient-visible case;
- strict excess is equivalent to an interior critical point of \(\Delta\);
- equality \(B_1=B_0+B_2\) is only sterile touching. 

So the live issue is exactly whether \(\Delta'\) can vanish in the interior.

Now write
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]
This suggests introducing the slope-ratio observable
\[
R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
\]
Then
\[
\Delta'(q)=0 \quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad R(q)=1.
\]

So strict excess is equivalent to an interior crossing of the level \(R=1\).

That is already a cleaner one-variable reduction:
\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess } \Longleftrightarrow \text{ the outer-run slope ratio }R(q)\text{ hits }1\text{ in the interior.}
}
\]

The next theorem target should therefore be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The outer-run slope ratio }R(q)\textbf{ is one-sided on the strict three-run interval.}
}
\]

If that holds, strict excess is dead immediately.

Now compute its logarithmic derivative:
\[
\frac{R'(q)}{R(q)}
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_0(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q)))^2}
-
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_2(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)))^2}.
\]
Equivalently, using
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
this becomes
\[
\frac{R'(q)}{R(q)}
=
\Xi(\phi_0(q))\,\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))
-
\Xi(\phi_2(q))\,\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So there are now two nested attack routes.

The stronger one is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Value-space descent of }
u\mapsto \Xi(u)\widehat Q'(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^2}
\textbf{ across the outer runs.}
}
\]

If this quantity descends across same values on the outer runs, then
\[
\frac{R'}{R}=0,
\]
so \(R\) is constant on the common value interval. Then either \(R\neq 1\) everywhere, and strict excess is impossible, or \(R\equiv 1\), which means same-value same-slope everywhere and should collapse into the equality/touching regime rather than the strict case.

That is already a very nice fork.

But there is a weaker, probably more attackable target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{One-crossing theorem for }R(q)\textbf{ with endpoint sign control.}
}
\]

Because if one can show \(R\) is monotone, or even just that \(R-1\) has at most one zero, then the run-amplitude data \(B_0,B_1,B_2\) may be enough to rule out that zero in the strict case and allow it only in the equality case.

This is where the lobe-amplitude theorem should re-enter. The three-run data are exactly the data of the outer/middle lobe amplitudes, and the equality/strict inequality split
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2
\quad\text{vs.}\quad
B_1>B_0+B_2
\]
should be reflected in the endpoint behavior of \(R\) or \(\Delta'\). The natural conjecture is:

\[
\boxed{
B_1=B_0+B_2 \Longleftrightarrow \text{borderline endpoint sign pattern for }R-1,
}
\]
whereas
\[
\boxed{
B_1>B_0+B_2 \Longrightarrow R-1 \text{ keeps a strict sign on }I.
}
\]

If that can be proved, strict excess is gone.

So the concrete next theorem I would now try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{In the strict three-run quotient-visible model, }R(q)-1\textbf{ has no interior zero.}
}
\]

This is equivalent to
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{No same-value same-slope coincidence across the separated outer runs in the strict case.}
}
\]

It is a bit better than the earlier self-intersection phrasing because it turns the problem into a scalar one-variable question about \(R\), and the derivatives of \(R\) are explicit in terms of \(\Xi\) and \(\widehat Q'\).

There is also a nice fallback statement if full no-zero is too hard:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{If }R(q_*)=1\textbf{ at an interior point, then necessarily }B_1=B_0+B_2.
}
\]

That would still kill strict excess.

So the attack stack on this lane is now, in order of likely tractability:

1. Prove endpoint sign control for \(R-1\) from the three-lobe amplitudes \(B_0,B_1,B_2\).
2. Prove \(R-1\) cannot cross zero in the strict case.
3. Or, weaker but enough, prove any zero of \(R-1\) forces equality-case touching.

That is the sharpest next direction I see on the strict-excess fixed-shear front.
