## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:05:57)

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the strict-excess fixed-shear fork is now best attacked through the **endpoint behavior of the outer-run slope ratio**
\[
R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))},
\]
because the whole strict-excess question has already been reduced to whether \(R\) can hit \(1\) in the interior. Indeed,
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
so
\[
\Delta'(q)=0 \iff R(q)=1.
\]
Thus strict excess is exactly the existence of an interior zero of \(R-1\). That is now the clean scalar formulation of the live fixed-shear problem. 

So the next thing to squeeze is not \(R\) everywhere at once, but its **endpoint signs**.

Let \(I=[q_-,q_+]\) be the common value interval of the two outer runs. At the endpoints, one or both inverse branches are approaching adjacent run boundaries. The consolidation says the live data are the three run amplitudes \(B_0,B_1,B_2\), and the only genuinely live three-run case is
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
while
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2
\]
is only sterile touching. That strongly suggests that the endpoint behavior of \(R-1\) should be encoded exactly by the sign of
\[
B_1-(B_0+B_2).
\]
So the next theorem target I would attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Endpoint-sign theorem for }R-1.
}
\]

A good form would be:

> On the common value interval of the two outer runs, the endpoint signs of \(R(q)-1\) are governed by the lobe-amplitude defect
> \[
> \Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2).
> \]
> In particular, in the strict case \(\Lambda>0\), \(R-1\) has the same strict sign at both endpoints, while in the equality case \(\Lambda=0\), one gets the borderline touching pattern.

If that theorem lands, then the fixed-shear lane is almost done, because one would only need a one-crossing or monotonicity statement for \(R\) to exclude an interior hit in the strict case.

Why this looks plausible is that the run amplitudes are exactly the integrated gap data of the three-lobe model, while \(R\) is the first-derivative version of the same outer-branch transport comparison. The equality
\[
B_1=B_0+B_2
\]
should be the exact balance condition under which the two outer branches can just touch in value-space transport, while strict inequality should separate them on one side. So it is very natural that \(R-1\) “remembers” the same balance defect at the interval endpoints. 

There is also a more differential way to phrase the same target. We already have
\[
\frac{R'(q)}{R(q)}
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_0(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q)))^2}
-
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_2(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)))^2}.
\]
So if one could prove that this logarithmic derivative has a fixed sign, then \(R\) is monotone and the endpoint-sign theorem would immediately imply no interior zero of \(R-1\) in the strict case. That yields the nested route:

1. prove endpoint sign control for \(R-1\) from \(B_1-(B_0+B_2)\);
2. prove monotonicity or at-most-one-crossing for \(R\);
3. conclude strict excess is impossible when \(B_1>B_0+B_2\).

This now looks like the cleanest one-variable attack on the fixed-shear front.

There is a weaker but still very useful theorem target if full endpoint-sign control is hard:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Any interior zero of }R-1\textbf{ forces the equality case }B_1=B_0+B_2.
}
\]

This is a direct scalar version of the earlier “same-value same-slope implies equality-case touching” statement, and I think it is the most realistic next result. It avoids proving all of monotonicity and instead just ties a hypothetical interior hit to the borderline lobe-amplitude relation.

So my current best attack stack on the strict-excess fork is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess} \Longrightarrow \exists q_*\in I:\ R(q_*)=1,
}
\]
then try to prove
\[
\boxed{
R(q_*)=1 \Longrightarrow B_1=B_0+B_2.
}
\]

If that lands, the strict three-run case is dead.

That is the sharpest next direction I see on this front.
## User (2026-04-21 23:07:02)

continue your math attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:07:25)

_Thought for 21s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the fixed-shear strict-excess fork is now best attacked by moving one step **below** the endpoint-sign question and looking for an exact identity that ties an interior zero of
\[
R(q)-1
\]
to the three-run amplitude defect
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2).
\]

That is the right next squeeze, because the consolidated picture has already reduced the live strict-excess case to a one-variable inverse-branch problem on the outer runs. 

## 1. Repackage the strict-excess problem one more time

On the common value interval \(I\) of the two outer runs, let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
be the outer inverse branches of \(\widehat Q\), and define
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
The current reduction says:

- strict excess is the only genuinely live three-run case;
- strict excess is equivalent to an interior critical point of \(\Delta\);
- equality
  \[
  B_1=B_0+B_2
  \]
  is only sterile touching. 

Now define the outer-run slope ratio
\[
R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
\]
Then
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
so
\[
\Delta'(q)=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
R(q)=1.
\]

So the strict-excess fork is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Does }R(q)-1\text{ vanish in the interior of }I\ ?
}
\]

That part is already very clean.

## 2. The next identity to attack

The right next thing is to integrate \(\Delta''\) against the affine Green kernel on \(I=[q_-,q_+]\).

For any \(C^2\) function \(f\) on \([q_-,q_+]\), one has the exact identity
\[
f(q)-\ell_f(q)
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,f''(s)\,ds,
\]
where \(\ell_f\) is the affine interpolant between the endpoint values and \(G\) is the standard nonnegative Green kernel for the Dirichlet second-derivative problem.

Apply this to
\[
f=\Delta.
\]
Then
\[
\Delta(q)-\ell_\Delta(q)
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,\Delta''(s)\,ds.
\]
Since
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3},
\]
this becomes
\[
\boxed{
\Delta(q)-\ell_\Delta(q)
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,\bigl(\Xi(\phi_0(s))-\Xi(\phi_2(s))\bigr)\,ds.
}
\]

This is a very useful formula. It says:

- if inverse curvature descends, i.e.
  \[
  \Xi(\phi_0)=\Xi(\phi_2),
  \]
  then \(\Delta\) is affine;
- more generally, any deviation of \(\Delta\) from its endpoint chord is controlled exactly by the outer-run inverse-curvature mismatch.

That is the right analytic observable for strict excess.

## 3. Why this helps with the three-lobe defect

The consolidated fixed-shear theorem says the whole three-run obstruction is governed by the lobe amplitudes \(B_0,B_1,B_2\), and the only live case is strict excess
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)>0.
\]
Equality \(\Lambda=0\) is only touching. 

So the natural next conjecture is:

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda
\text{ is represented by a weighted endpoint-chord defect of }\Delta.
}
\]

In other words, there should exist a positive weight \(w(q)\) on \(I\) such that
\[
\Lambda
=
\int_I w(q)\,\bigl(\Delta(q)-\ell_\Delta(q)\bigr)\,dq.
\]

If that formula can be proved, then substituting the Green-kernel representation above gives
\[
\Lambda
=
\int_I \widetilde w(s)\,\bigl(\Xi(\phi_0(s))-\Xi(\phi_2(s))\bigr)\,ds
\]
for some positive weight \(\widetilde w\).

That would be a major advance, because then:

- equality-case touching \(\Lambda=0\) corresponds to exact weighted balance of inverse-curvature mismatch;
- strict excess \(\Lambda>0\) means a definite signed imbalance of the outer-run inverse curvature.

And then a same-value same-slope coincidence \(R(q_*)=1\) would have to coexist with that imbalance. That looks much harder, and plausibly impossible.

So the concrete next theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Represent the three-lobe defect }\Lambda\textbf{ as a weighted chord-defect functional of }\Delta.
}
\]

This is, I think, the right next thing to try to prove.

## 4. Why this is better than directly proving monotonicity of \(R\)

Directly proving \(R\) monotone is attractive, but it may be too strong too early. The Green-kernel identity is softer. It turns the problem into:

- endpoint data of \(\Delta\),
- plus a weighted average of inverse-curvature mismatch.

That is much closer to the actual three-lobe amplitude model already present in the paper. It uses the quantity the fixed-shear theorem naturally controls, namely the run amplitudes \(B_j\), rather than asking for a global pointwise monotonicity miracle.

So the attack stack now looks like this:

First:
\[
\boxed{
\text{express }\Lambda=B_1-(B_0+B_2)\text{ as a positive weighted functional of }\Delta-\ell_\Delta.
}
\]

Second:
\[
\boxed{
\text{rewrite that as a positive weighted functional of }\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2).
}
\]

Third:
\[
\boxed{
\text{show an interior zero of }R-1\text{ is incompatible with }\Lambda>0.
}
\]

This is more aligned with the current consolidation than the earlier more abstract self-intersection language.

## 5. A useful intermediate consequence

Even before proving the full weighted-\(\Lambda\) formula, the Green-kernel identity gives a weaker but still valuable fork:

If
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(s))-\Xi(\phi_2(s))
\]
has a fixed sign on \(I\), then
\[
\Delta-\ell_\Delta
\]
has the same sign, because the Green kernel is nonnegative.

That means \(\Delta\) lies strictly on one side of its endpoint chord. A function that lies strictly on one side of its chord is very restricted in how its derivative can vanish in the interior. In particular, if the endpoint slopes already reflect the strict three-run geometry, this may be enough to rule out an interior critical point of \(\Delta\).

So a still weaker, perhaps more attackable theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Outer-run inverse-curvature mismatch has fixed sign on }I.
}
\]

If that holds, then \(\Delta\) has one-sided curvature relative to its chord, and strict excess becomes much harder to realize.

## 6. The sharpest current conjecture on this front

So the strongest next conjecture I would now put forward is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{On the strict three-run quotient-visible model, the lobe-amplitude defect }\Lambda
\textbf{ is a positive weighted functional of the outer inverse-curvature mismatch.}
}
\]

And the most useful consequence would be:

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda>0
\quad\Longrightarrow\quad
R(q)-1\text{ cannot vanish in the interior.}
}
\]

That would kill strict excess outright.

## 7. What I would try next

The next concrete sub-attack is:

1. identify how the run amplitudes \(B_0,B_1,B_2\) are expressed in the outer inverse-branch variables;
2. isolate the combination
   \[
   \Lambda=B_1-(B_0+B_2);
   \]
3. try to rewrite it as an integral functional of \(\Delta\), ideally of \(\Delta-\ell_\Delta\);
4. then substitute the Green-kernel identity above.

That is the sharpest, most aligned next attack I see on the strict-excess fixed-shear front.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:09:23)

_Thought for 18s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the strict-excess fixed-shear fork is now best attacked by moving from the pointwise condition
\[
R(q)=1
\]
to an **integrated convexity defect** of the branch-gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

That is a better object because the three-run model already speaks in terms of integrated lobe amplitudes \(B_0,B_1,B_2\), not raw pointwise slopes. So the right next move is to match the local inverse-branch analysis to that integrated amplitude language.

## 1. The exact one-variable identities we already have

On the common value interval \(I=[q_-,q_+]\) of the two outer inverse branches,
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q,\qquad i=0,2,
\]
and
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]

Then
\[
\phi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))},
\qquad
\phi_i''(q)=-\,\Xi(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

Also, with
\[
R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))},
\]
we have
\[
\Delta'(q)=0\iff R(q)=1.
\]
So strict excess is exactly the existence of an interior zero of \(R-1\). That part is now completely clean. 

## 2. The Green-kernel identity is the right transport formula

For any \(C^2\) function \(f\) on \([q_-,q_+]\),
\[
f(q)-\ell_f(q)
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,f''(s)\,ds,
\]
where \(\ell_f\) is the affine interpolant of the endpoint values and \(G\ge 0\) is the Dirichlet Green kernel.

Applying this to \(f=\Delta\),
\[
\boxed{
\Delta(q)-\ell_\Delta(q)
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,\bigl(\Xi(\phi_0(s))-\Xi(\phi_2(s))\bigr)\,ds.
}
\]

This is, in my view, the right central identity on the fixed-shear fork. It says the failure of the outer branch-gap to be affine in value space is controlled exactly by the inverse-curvature mismatch across the outer runs.

So the strict-excess lane has now become:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the inverse-curvature mismatch force an interior critical point of }\Delta\text{ while the three-run amplitudes satisfy }B_1>B_0+B_2\ ?
}
\]

That is much tighter than anything earlier.

## 3. The new conjectural bridge to the three-lobe defect

The next theorem I would now attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The three-lobe defect }\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\textbf{ is a positive weighted functional of }\Delta-\ell_\Delta.
}
\]

In other words, I want a formula of the shape
\[
\Lambda
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} w(q)\,\bigl(\Delta(q)-\ell_\Delta(q)\bigr)\,dq
\]
with
\[
w(q)\ge 0,
\qquad
w\not\equiv 0.
\]

If that formula is true, then inserting the Green-kernel representation gives
\[
\Lambda
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} \widetilde w(s)\,\bigl(\Xi(\phi_0(s))-\Xi(\phi_2(s))\bigr)\,ds
\]
for another nonnegative weight \(\widetilde w\).

That would be huge, because then the lobe-amplitude obstruction becomes a weighted inverse-curvature mismatch obstruction. Equality
\[
\Lambda=0
\]
would correspond to perfect weighted balance, and strict excess
\[
\Lambda>0
\]
would force a definite signed imbalance.

This is the first really natural way I see to tie the integrated lobe-amplitude model to the local inverse-branch calculus.

## 4. Why this is plausible

The consolidation already says the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model is governed by the run amplitudes \(B_j\), and that the only live three-run condition is
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
with equality giving only touching. 

But \(\Delta\) is exactly the outer-run separation in value space. So if the three-lobe amplitudes are the integrated transport content of the same-value geometry, it is very natural that the defect
\[
\Lambda=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\]
should be expressible as some weighted convexity/concavity defect of \(\Delta\) relative to its chord.

That is the heuristic:
- equality-case touching means \(\Delta\) is “just affine enough” on \(I\),
- strict excess means \(\Delta\) bends enough away from its chord to create a real interior critical point.

So a positive weighted chord-defect formula for \(\Lambda\) is exactly the right thing to look for.

## 5. A weaker but still useful next theorem

Even before proving the full weighted-\(\Lambda\) formula, there is a weaker theorem that would already help a lot:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The inverse-curvature mismatch }
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\textbf{ has fixed sign on }I.
}
\]

If that holds, then by the Green-kernel identity
\[
\Delta-\ell_\Delta
\]
has that same sign on all of \(I\), because \(G\ge 0\).

So \(\Delta\) lies strictly on one side of its endpoint chord. A function that lies strictly on one side of its chord is highly constrained in how its derivative can behave in the interior. In particular, once one combines that with the endpoint behavior dictated by the strict three-run model, it becomes much harder for \(\Delta'\) to vanish inside.

So a still more attackable next target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Outer-run inverse-curvature mismatch has one sign on the common value interval.}
}
\]

This would not by itself finish the lane, but it would be a serious tightening.

## 6. A particularly sharp scalar reformulation

There is also a very clean scalar quantity to track:
\[
R(q)=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
\]
Its logarithmic derivative is
\[
\frac{R'(q)}{R(q)}
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_0(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q)))^2}
-
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_2(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)))^2}.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
\frac{R'}{R}
=
\Xi(\phi_0)\,\widehat Q'(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2)\,\widehat Q'(\phi_2).
\]

So another nested theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The logarithmic slope-ratio derivative }R'/R\textbf{ has fixed sign on }I.
}
\]

If that holds, then \(R\) is monotone and can hit \(1\) at most once. Combined with endpoint control coming from the lobe-amplitude defect, that may already be enough to force:
- no hit in the strict case,
- at most borderline touching in the equality case.

So the strict-excess fork has now been reduced to a very crisp scalar analysis problem.

## 7. The best next theorem I would try to prove

If I had to pick one statement to attack next, it would be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Any interior zero of }R(q)-1\textbf{ forces the equality case }B_1=B_0+B_2.
}
\]

That is weaker than proving \(R-1\) has no interior zero outright, but it is exactly enough. It says:

- same-value same-slope coincidence across the separated outer runs is not compatible with strict excess;
- it belongs only to the sterile touching boundary.

That is the most natural next theorem because it matches the current three-run/lobe-amplitude language perfectly.

## 8. The current attack stack on this front

So the fixed-shear strict-excess lane now looks like this:

First:
\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess } \Longleftrightarrow \exists q_*\in I:\ R(q_*)=1
}
\]

Second, try to prove one of:

\[
\boxed{
R(q_*)=1 \Longrightarrow B_1=B_0+B_2,
}
\]

or more structurally,

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\text{ is a positive weighted functional of }\Delta-\ell_\Delta,
}
\]

or weaker,

\[
\boxed{
\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2)\text{ has fixed sign on }I.
}
\]

Any of those would materially tighten or kill the strict-excess fork.

That is the sharpest continuation I see right now.

Here is the next honest push.

I think the fixed-shear strict-excess fork is now best attacked by turning the three-run amplitude defect
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\]
into an **endpoint-corrected integral of the branch-gap slope**. That is the cleanest way to connect the lobe-amplitude model to the inverse-branch calculus.

We already have the outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
on the common value interval \(I=[q_-,q_+]\), and the branch gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Also,
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
so strict excess is equivalent to an interior zero of \(\Delta'\), equivalently of
\[
R(q)-1,\qquad
R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
\]

The next thing I would try to prove is that the amplitude defect \(\Lambda\) is not some unrelated combinatorial quantity, but is represented by a positive weighted integral of \(\Delta'\) or, better, of the chord defect of \(\Delta\).

The most natural formula to aim for is something like
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} w(q)\,\bigl(\Delta(q)-\ell_\Delta(q)\bigr)\,dq
}
\]
with \(w(q)\ge 0\), where \(\ell_\Delta\) is the affine interpolant of \(\Delta\) at the endpoints.

Why this is the right target is that we already have the Green-kernel identity
\[
\Delta(q)-\ell_\Delta(q)
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,\Delta''(s)\,ds
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,\bigl(\Xi(\phi_0(s))-\Xi(\phi_2(s))\bigr)\,ds,
\]
with
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So if \(\Lambda\) really is a positive weighted functional of \(\Delta-\ell_\Delta\), then \(\Lambda\) becomes a positive weighted functional of the outer-run inverse-curvature mismatch
\[
\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2).
\]

That would be a major gain, because then:

- equality-case touching \(\Lambda=0\) would correspond to exact weighted balance of inverse-curvature mismatch;
- strict excess \(\Lambda>0\) would force a definite signed imbalance;
- and a same-value same-slope coincidence \(R(q_*)=1\) would have to coexist with that imbalance.

This is a much sharper analytic picture than just “\(R\) hits \(1\).”

There is also a weaker and probably more attackable intermediate theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The sign of }\Lambda\textbf{ controls the endpoint signs of }\Delta'-\ell_\Delta'.
}
\]

But since \(\ell_\Delta'\) is constant, this is really about controlling the endpoint behavior of \(\Delta'\) itself. If one could prove that
\[
\Lambda>0
\]
forces \(\Delta'\) to have the same strict sign at both endpoints, then any one-crossing or monotonicity theorem for \(\Delta'\) would kill strict excess immediately.

So I now see the fixed-shear attack stack as:

First, prove an integral representation of \(\Lambda\) in terms of \(\Delta-\ell_\Delta\), or at least endpoint data of \(\Delta'\).

Second, combine that with the exact identity
\[
\Delta''=\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2)
\]
to express \(\Lambda\) as a weighted inverse-curvature mismatch.

Third, show that if \(R(q_*)=1\) somewhere in the interior, then necessarily \(\Lambda=0\), i.e.
\[
\boxed{
R(q_*)=1 \Longrightarrow B_1=B_0+B_2.
}
\]

That would be enough: it would say same-value same-slope coincidence belongs only to the sterile touching boundary, never to strict excess.

There is one more useful scalar quantity here:
\[
\frac{R'(q)}{R(q)}
=
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_0(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q)))^2}
-
\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_2(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)))^2}.
\]
So if I cannot get the full weighted-\(\Lambda\) formula, the next-best theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The logarithmic slope-ratio derivative }R'/R\textbf{ has fixed sign on }I.
}
\]

That would make \(R\) monotone, hence \(R-1\) could hit \(1\) at most once, and then endpoint information from \(\Lambda\) would likely be enough to exclude the hit in the strict case.

So the sharpest next concrete target I would now attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Represent the three-lobe defect }\Lambda\textbf{ as a positive weighted chord-defect functional of }\Delta.
}
\]

If that lands, the strict-excess fork gets converted into a clean inverse-curvature mismatch problem, and I think it becomes much more tractable.

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the fixed-shear strict-excess fork can now be attacked through a **Sturm-style one-crossing strategy** for the outer-branch gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and this is probably the most concrete route we have on that front.

The reason is that the whole strict-excess problem has already been reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess } \Longleftrightarrow \exists q_*\in (q_-,q_+) \text{ with } \Delta'(q_*)=0
}
\]

equivalently,

\[
\boxed{
\exists q_*\in (q_-,q_+) \text{ with } R(q_*)=1,
\qquad
R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
}
\]

So the right next theorem is no longer “more geometry.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Show that }\Delta' \textbf{ has at most one sign change, and that the endpoint sign pattern in the strict case forbids a zero.}
}
\]

That splits naturally into two sub-targets.

## 1. A one-crossing theorem for \(\Delta'\)

We already have
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]

So if one can prove that
\[
q\longmapsto \Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
has fixed sign, then \(\Delta'\) is monotone on the whole overlap interval \(I\). In that case \(\Delta'\) can vanish at most once.

That gives the first crisp theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Outer-run inverse-curvature mismatch has fixed sign on the common value interval.}
}
\]

This is weaker than full descent of \(\Xi\), but still enough to force a one-crossing principle for \(\Delta'\).

If that feels too strong, an even weaker but still useful target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The inverse-curvature mismatch is one-sign after a single possible transition.}
}
\]

That would still give strong control on the number of zeros of \(\Delta'\).

## 2. Endpoint sign control from the lobe defect

The second thing needed is to connect the three-lobe amplitude defect
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\]
to the signs of \(\Delta'\) at the two endpoints \(q_-,q_+\).

The natural conjecture is:

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda>0 \Longrightarrow \Delta'(q_-) \text{ and } \Delta'(q_+) \text{ have the same strict sign,}
}
\]

while

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda=0 \Longrightarrow \text{borderline endpoint-touching sign pattern.}
}
\]

If this is true, then combined with monotonicity or one-crossing of \(\Delta'\), strict excess dies: a function with the same strict sign at both endpoints and at most one crossing cannot have an interior zero.

So the second sharp theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Endpoint-sign theorem for }\Delta' \textbf{ in terms of } \Lambda=B_1-(B_0+B_2).
}
\]

This is, I think, more attackable than trying to prove a full positive weighted formula for \(\Lambda\) immediately.

## 3. Why this is promising

This route matches the consolidated picture very closely:

- the lobe theorem has already compressed the live exact fixed-shear case to the strict three-run excess
  \[
  B_1>B_0+B_2;
  \]
- strict excess is already exactly the inverse-branch-gap problem;
- and \(\Delta'\) is the scalar quantity whose zero detects the obstruction. 

So the next theorem should really be about \(\Delta'\), not about another layer of local branch structure.

## 4. A useful exact identity for endpoint analysis

There is also a handy integral identity:
\[
\Delta'(q)-\Delta'(q_-)
=
\int_{q_-}^{q}\bigl(\Xi(\phi_0(s))-\Xi(\phi_2(s))\bigr)\,ds.
\]

So if the inverse-curvature mismatch has fixed sign, then \(\Delta'\) moves monotonically away from its left endpoint value. Similarly from the right endpoint. This is exactly the setup where endpoint sign control would be decisive.

That suggests the real local one-variable package is now:

\[
\boxed{
\text{endpoint signs of }\Delta' \quad + \quad \text{sign control on }\Delta''.
}
\]

If both are obtained, strict excess is gone.

## 5. The next exact theorem I would now try to prove

If I had to choose one statement to attack next, it would be this:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{In the strict three-run quotient-visible model, }\Delta'\textbf{ has no interior zero.}
}
\]

But the proof route I would pursue is the two-step version:

1. prove a one-crossing/monotonicity theorem for \(\Delta'\) from the sign of \(\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2)\);
2. prove endpoint sign control for \(\Delta'\) from the lobe defect \(\Lambda\).

That is the sharpest concrete continuation I see on the fixed-shear front.

## 6. Relation to the package-level lane

This also has a nice strategic consequence.

If this one-variable strict-excess fork is killed, then the exact fixed-shear quotient-visible lane is reduced even further, and what remains on the mixed/package side is even more clearly the nonlocal/provenance-sensitive piece: collision-functoriality, same reduced image germ, or the quotient-septic obstruction.

So this is still a very valuable place to attack: it may completely remove the last genuinely geometric-looking piece of the fixed-shear front.

That is where I would keep pushing next.

I think the fixed-shear strict-excess fork is now best attacked by trying to prove a **comparison principle for the outer inverse branches** that makes \(\Delta'\) one-sided.

We already have the exact scalar setup.

On the common value interval \(I=[q_-,q_+]\) of the two outer runs,
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]
So strict excess is exactly:
\[
\exists q_*\in (q_-,q_+) \text{ with } \Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
R(q_*):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q_*))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_*))}=1.
\]
That reduction is already part of the consolidated picture. 

The next thing to exploit is that the inverse branches solve the same first-order ODE in value space:
\[
\phi_i'(q)=F(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
F(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]
So \(\Delta\) itself satisfies the exact nonlinear equation
\[
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)).
\]

Now apply the mean value theorem in the \(u\)-variable. For each \(q\in I\), there exists some intermediate point \(\xi(q)\) between \(\phi_0(q)\) and \(\phi_2(q)\) such that
\[
\Delta'(q)=F'(\xi(q))\,\Delta(q).
\]
Since
\[
F'(u)=-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2},
\]
this becomes
\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q)= -\,\frac{\widehat Q''(\xi(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\xi(q)))^2}\,\Delta(q).
}
\]

This is a very useful formula. It says the sign of \(\Delta'\) is controlled by the sign of the scalar
\[
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\]
sampled somewhere between the two outer inverse branches.

Because \(\Delta(q)>0\) on the overlap interval, an interior zero of \(\Delta'\) can occur only if \(H\) changes sign somewhere between the outer runs.

So the strict-excess fork is now reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can }H(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}\text{ change sign across the region swept out between the outer runs?}
}
\]

That is sharper than the earlier \(R\)-monotonicity target. It turns the problem into a direct sign question for a single scalar field in \(u\)-space.

This suggests the next concrete theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Sign theorem for }H(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\textbf{ on the outer-run transport strip.}
}
\]

If one can show \(H\) has fixed sign on the region between the outer runs over the common value interval, then \(\Delta'\) has fixed sign on \(I\), hence strict excess is impossible.

That is a much more local theorem than full inverse-curvature descent, and probably more attackable.

There is also a slightly weaker version that would still be enough:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The sign of }H\textbf{ on the interval between }\phi_0(q)\textbf{ and }\phi_2(q)\textbf{ is constant in }q.
}
\]

Because then \(\Delta'\) would still keep one sign.

This route also meshes well with the three-lobe amplitude picture. The consolidation says the only live three-run case is
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)>0,
\]
while equality is only touching. 

So the natural conjecture is that the strict sign of \(\Lambda\) should force a strict sign for \(H\) on the transport strip, while \(\Lambda=0\) should correspond to the borderline case where \(H\) can vanish and only touching remains.

That yields an even more concrete target:

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda>0 \Longrightarrow H \text{ keeps a strict sign on the outer-run transport strip.}
}
\]

If that statement is true, the strict-excess fork is dead immediately.

There is one more gain from the mean-value representation. Differentiate once more:
\[
\Delta''(q)=H'(\xi(q))\,\xi'(q)\,\Delta(q)+H(\xi(q))\,\Delta'(q).
\]
So if \(H\) has fixed sign and modest variation, \(\Delta\) satisfies a first-order scalar comparison equation of the form
\[
\Delta'=\sigma(q)\Delta,\qquad \sigma(q)\text{ fixed sign},
\]
which is exactly the regime where no interior critical point can appear. In that sense, the whole strict-excess fork has become a comparison-ODE problem for \(\Delta\).

So the attack stack on this front is now:

First:
\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q)= -\,H(\xi(q))\,\Delta(q)
\quad\text{for some }\xi(q)\in(\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q)).
}
\]

Then try to prove one of:

\[
\boxed{
H \text{ has fixed sign on the outer-run transport strip,}
}
\]

or at least

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda>0 \Longrightarrow H \text{ has fixed sign on that strip.}
}
\]

Either one would kill strict excess.

So the next concrete theorem I would now try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Strict three-run sign theorem: }
\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\text{ has fixed sign between the outer runs when }B_1>B_0+B_2.
}
\]

That is the sharpest next scalar attack I see on the fixed-shear front.
## User (2026-04-21 23:13:46)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:13:58)

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the best continuation on the strict-excess fixed-shear fork is to refine the sign-theorem target for
\[
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\]
into a **strip comparison principle**.

The last step gave the exact mean-value identity
\[
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q))
\]
with
\[
F(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
and therefore
\[
\Delta'(q)=-\,H(\xi(q))\,\Delta(q)
\]
for some intermediate point
\[
\xi(q)\in(\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q)).
\]
Since \(\Delta(q)>0\) on the common value interval, the sign of \(\Delta'\) is controlled by the sign of \(H\) somewhere in the strip between the outer runs.

So the right next question is no longer “is \(H\) globally signed?” but:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can }H\text{ change sign inside the outer-run transport strip in the strict three-run case?}
}
\]

That is sharper and more plausible to attack.

## 1. The transport strip

Define the transport strip
\[
\mathcal S:=\{\,u:\phi_0(q)\le u\le \phi_2(q)\text{ for some }q\in I\,\},
\]
where \(I=[q_-,q_+]\) is the common value interval of the two outer runs.

The strict-excess case requires an interior zero of \(\Delta'\), hence by the mean-value identity it requires that \(H\) either vanishes or changes sign somewhere in \(\mathcal S\).

So the exact next theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Strict three-run strip-sign theorem. }
B_1>B_0+B_2 \Longrightarrow H \text{ has fixed sign on }\mathcal S.
}
\]

If that theorem lands, strict excess dies immediately, because then \(\Delta'\) keeps one sign on \(I\).

## 2. Why this is better than a pointwise theorem on \(\Xi\)

Earlier I attacked via
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
That is still natural, but \(H\) is actually the more relevant scalar for the one-variable ODE, because
\[
\phi_i'(q)=F(\phi_i(q)),\qquad F'(u)=-H(u).
\]
So \(H\) measures the monotonicity/convexity of the inverse-branch vector field itself, which is exactly what governs whether two solutions \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) can approach, separate, or develop an interior critical point in their gap.

In other words, the strict-excess fork has now become a **comparison theorem for two solutions of the same scalar ODE**
\[
\phi'=F(\phi),
\]
and \(H=-F'\) is the correct coefficient.

That is a useful conceptual simplification.

## 3. A local comparison lemma that should be true

Suppose \(H\) has fixed sign on \(\mathcal S\). Then \(F\) is monotone on \(\mathcal S\). Since
\[
\phi_0'(q)=F(\phi_0(q)),\qquad \phi_2'(q)=F(\phi_2(q)),
\]
and \(\phi_2(q)>\phi_0(q)\), monotonicity of \(F\) implies
\[
\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
\]
has fixed sign on \(I\). But that difference is exactly \(\Delta'(q)\). So:

\[
\boxed{
H \text{ one-sign on }\mathcal S \Longrightarrow \Delta' \text{ one-sign on }I.
}
\]

This is the clean comparison lemma I would now regard as established modulo the sign theorem for \(H\).

So the whole strict-excess lane has become:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess can survive only if }H\text{ changes sign in the outer-run strip.}
}
\]

That is already a strong compression.

## 4. How the three-lobe defect should enter

The consolidated update says the only genuinely live three-run case is
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)>0,
\]
while
\[
\Lambda=0
\]
is sterile touching. So the natural conjecture is:

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda>0 \Longrightarrow H \text{ keeps a strict sign on }\mathcal S,
}
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\Lambda=0 \Longrightarrow H \text{ may vanish, corresponding only to touching.}
}
\]

This is the best current way I know to connect the integrated lobe-amplitude model to the local inverse-branch ODE.

What makes this plausible is that \(\Lambda\) is the unique live scalar defect left by the sign-run compression. If there were an interior sign change of \(H\) in the strict case, that would be a second scalar instability in the same quotient-visible model, which would be surprising given how hard the model has already been compressed.

That is not a proof, but it is a good heuristic for why the sign theorem for \(H\) is the right next target.

## 5. A weaker theorem that would still help a lot

Even if full one-sign control of \(H\) is too strong, a weaker theorem would still materially tighten the fork:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{The zero set of }H\textbf{ intersects each same-value segment }[\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q)]\textbf{ in at most one point.}
}
\]

Then \(\Delta'\) could change sign at most once. Combined with endpoint sign information from \(\Lambda\), that might still be enough to exclude an interior zero in the strict case.

So there are really two nested goals now:

First, strongest:
\[
\boxed{H \text{ has fixed sign on }\mathcal S.}
\]

Second, fallback:
\[
\boxed{H \text{ has at most one crossing on each outer-run segment.}}
\]

Either would be useful.

## 6. A more geometric restatement

This can also be phrased in geometric language that may be easier to attack.

Since
\[
F(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
the condition that \(H=-F'\) has fixed sign means \(F\) is monotone on the strip. So the strict-excess fork can be restated as:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the inverse-slope field }F(u)=1/\widehat Q'(u)\text{ fail monotonicity between the outer runs in the strict case?}
}
\]

If \(F\) is monotone there, the two outer inverse branches can never develop a critical-point gap. So another clean theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Outer-strip inverse-slope monotonicity in the strict three-run case.}
}
\]

That is exactly equivalent to the sign theorem for \(H\).

## 7. The sharpest current theorem target

So the next statement I would now actually try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{In the strict three-run quotient-visible model, }
u\mapsto \frac1{\widehat Q'(u)}
\textbf{ is monotone on the transport strip between the two outer runs.}
}
\]

From that it follows immediately that
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
\]
has fixed sign, so \(\Delta\) has no interior critical point, and strict excess is impossible.

That is, I think, the sharpest scalar attack currently available on the fixed-shear front.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:17:37)

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the cleanest way forward on the strict-excess fixed-shear fork is to stop asking for a global sign theorem on
\[
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\]
all at once, and instead derive a **barrier principle from the middle run**.

That is the most natural place where the three-run amplitude data should actually enter.

Recall the current one-variable reduction. On the common value interval \(I\) of the two outer runs, the inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) satisfy
\[
\phi_i'(q)=F(\phi_i(q)),\qquad F(u):=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
and the gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
\]
obeys
\[
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)).
\]
So strict excess is exactly the existence of an interior zero of \(\Delta'\), equivalently an interior same-value same-slope coincidence across the outer runs. The current strict-excess fork is therefore:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can }F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q))\text{ vanish in the interior?}
}
\]

Now, by the mean-value theorem, for each \(q\in I\) there is \(\xi(q)\in(\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q))\) such that
\[
\Delta'(q)=F'(\xi(q))\,\Delta(q)=-H(\xi(q))\,\Delta(q).
\]
Since \(\Delta(q)>0\), the sign of \(\Delta'\) is the sign of \(-H\) somewhere along the same-value segment between the outer runs. So the strict-excess obstruction is really:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Does the same-value segment between the outer runs cross a zero of }H\text{ for some }q\in I?
}
\]

That is sharper than the earlier “sign theorem on the whole strip.” It says we only need to control \(H\) on the **family of same-value chords** joining the two outer runs.

This suggests the next theorem target.

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Same-value chord barrier theorem.}
}
\]

For every common value \(q\in I\), the chord
\[
[\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q)]
\]
meets the zero set of \(H\) either never, or only in the equality-case touching regime \(B_1=B_0+B_2\). In particular, in the strict case
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
every same-value chord sees a fixed sign of \(H\), hence
\[
\Delta'(q)\neq 0
\]
for all \(q\in I\).

If that theorem lands, the strict-excess fork is dead.

Why this looks more attackable than a global strip theorem is that the same-value chords are exactly the geometric objects already singled out by the quotient-visible model. The lobe amplitudes \(B_0,B_1,B_2\) are measuring transport across the same-value structure, not arbitrary points in the strip. So asking about zeros of \(H\) on those chords is much more aligned with the current reduction than asking for full monotonicity of \(F\) on the entire strip. 

There is also a useful scalar reformulation.

Define the chord-sampled sign function
\[
\sigma(q):=\operatorname{sgn}(H(\xi(q))),
\]
where \(\xi(q)\) is any mean-value point with
\[
\Delta'(q)=-H(\xi(q))\,\Delta(q).
\]
If \(H\) has constant sign on each same-value chord, then \(\sigma(q)\) is well-defined and
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q)=-\sigma(q).
\]
So the problem becomes:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can }\sigma(q)\text{ change sign in the strict three-run case?}
}
\]

The natural conjecture is no: in the strict case, \(\sigma\) should be constant, while in the equality case it may hit zero, corresponding only to sterile touching.

This leads to an even more concrete intermediate target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Same-value chord sign theorem: }
B_1>B_0+B_2 \Longrightarrow \operatorname{sgn}H
\text{ is constant on every same-value chord.}
}
\]

That theorem would be enough.

There is one more angle that may help.

Because the middle run sits between the outer runs, each same-value chord crosses the whole “forbidden” middle lobe. If the lobe-amplitude defect
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\]
is strictly positive, then the middle run dominates the two outers in the exact way that made three-run overlap live. So it is plausible that \(\Lambda>0\) should act as a **barrier preventing the inverse-slope field \(F=1/\widehat Q'\) from having equal values at the two outer endpoints of a same-value chord**. In other words, strict excess should not just forbid a zero of \(\Delta'\) by accident; it should forbid equality
\[
F(\phi_0(q))=F(\phi_2(q))
\]
because the middle run forces one outer endpoint of the chord to lie strictly on one side of the inverse-slope profile. That suggests another equivalent target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Middle-run barrier theorem for }F=1/\widehat Q'.}
\]

In the strict three-run case, the middle run imposes a strict ordering
\[
F(\phi_0(q))\neq F(\phi_2(q))
\]
with fixed sign on the whole common value interval.

That is probably the most conceptual formulation of what we need.

So the attack stack on this front is now:

First:
\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess } \Longleftrightarrow \exists q\in I:\ F(\phi_2(q))=F(\phi_0(q)).
}
\]

Second, try to prove:
\[
\boxed{
B_1>B_0+B_2 \Longrightarrow
F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q))
\text{ keeps a strict sign on }I.
}
\]

Or equivalently:
\[
\boxed{
B_1>B_0+B_2 \Longrightarrow
\Delta'(q)\text{ keeps a strict sign on }I.
}
\]

That is the sharpest next theorem I see on the fixed-shear front.

If you want the most concrete next sub-attack, it is this: derive the endpoint values of
\[
F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q))
\]
at \(q=q_-\) and \(q=q_+\) in terms of the lobe amplitudes \(B_0,B_1,B_2\), and see whether the strict defect
\[
\Lambda=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\]
already forces those endpoint signs to agree. If it does, then any one-crossing or same-value-chord sign theorem finishes the lane.
## User (2026-04-21 23:21:08)

please give a complete recap of your whole attack along with new findings since most recent paper update. include theorems, no-gos, structural findings, negative findings, goodies, etc.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:22:03)

_Thought for 16s_

Here is the full recap of my attack since the most recent paper draft, including what now looks genuinely established, what is only conditional, what has been killed, and what I think the live endgame still is.

At the highest level, the main change is that the problem has become much cleaner. The geometry has been compressed hard enough that the remaining burden is no longer “find one more local sign argument.” The live space now splits into three narrow fronts: a nonmixed/global affine front where the remaining burden is RH-specific exclusion of zeros of monotone remote endpoint-gap functions; a mixed exact fixed-shear front where the remaining burden is a quotient-visible transport problem downstairs in \(u=t^2\); and a package-level / honest order-7 front where the real target is not raw septic equality but a provenance-sensitive statement such as same reduced image germ or collision-functoriality. The generator audit is part of that cleanup: no second explicit pointwise highest-new generator has appeared beyond \(r=q^{(7)}\), so if something survives, it should be transport/global/package-level rather than “one more local field.” turn65file1

## 1. The exact fixed-shear corner has been compressed much further than before

The most important positive gain is on the residual exact fixed-shear corner \(\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\}\). The overlap bad set is now sharply split: \((K_v=0,S\neq 0)\) is already mixed low-order, \((S=0,K_v\neq 0)\) transfers to nonmixed 4-point geometry, and only \((S=0)\cap(K_v=0)\) remains as the genuinely residual derivative-geometric corner. That corner is involutive / quotient-diagonal: repeated pairs are exactly \(t\leftrightarrow -t\), so there is no hidden local partner choice. Downstairs, the natural variable is \(u=t^2\), and the regularized transport state is quotient-visible. On the quartic–sextic rung the quotient-visible transport state has local rank one and there is no hidden finite-order reset. This is a real theorem-level compression of the mixed residual corner. turn65file1

More concretely, on the involutive fixed-shear branch the regularized quotient
\[
Q(t)=\frac{J_{u_5}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)}
\]
is even, so
\[
Q(t)=\widehat Q(t^2).
\]
Every parity-normalized finite transport jet descends to the quotient variable. On the first surviving rung \((m,n)=(4,6)\), equivalently \((r,e)=(2,0)\), one has \(\widehat Q'(0)=\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\), so \(\widehat Q\) is a local analytic coordinate in \(u\). That implies the whole finite quotient-visible transport state is locally a function of the single scalar \(Q\), hence has rank one. It also implies there is no third local reset in any finite descended transport state: the only local duplication is the involution \(t\leftrightarrow -t\). turn65file1

This already yields a strong negative result: if the exact fixed-shear corner is still genuinely live, then the obstruction cannot come from a second local transport variable, a hidden finite-order reset, or any two-pair model built only from the separate descended one-atom states with the natural merger law. In other words, the residual corner is no longer blocked by generic finite-state local geometry. 

## 2. The local chart was corrected, and that changed the attack materially

A second major gain was the correction of the local chart near a close bad 2-point core. The right local regime is not naive diagonal merger. It is the collision-cancellation chart
\[
m=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\lambda=-\frac{a_2}{a_1}\approx 1,
\]
or equivalently the swap-compatible coordinate
\[
\omega=\frac{\lambda-1}{\lambda+1}.
\]
In that chart, swap acts linearly by
\[
(m,\omega,\delta)\mapsto (m,-\omega,-\delta).
\]
This was not cosmetic. It changes the entire local blow-up structure: the relevant limiting regime is cancellation, not ordinary diagonal coincidence. turn65file1

Once that chart is used, a strong structural normal form appears. Any analytic swap-even local corrected defect \(E(m,\omega,\delta)\) with collision vanishing has no linear blown-up term. Its Taylor expansion starts at even total degree and in fact factors as
\[
E(m,\omega,\delta)=\delta^2\,\mathcal H(m,\kappa,\delta^2),
\qquad
\kappa=\frac{2\omega}{\delta}.
\]
So the whole ordinary analytic swap-invariant local scalar layer is already dead: the first surviving transverse layer is quadratic, projective, and one-parameter. Along the involutive fixed-shear branch even that quadratic layer is quotient-visible, and on the quartic–sextic rung its coefficient is expected to be a function of the single scalar \(Q\). So the live obstruction, if any, has already been pushed beyond the entire quadratic blow-up layer. This is one of the strongest structural gains from the later round of attack. 

This also gave a cleaner conditional closure target. One no longer needs a full package-level state-locality theorem to close the exact fixed-shear corner. It would already be enough to prove that the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects are analytic in the collision chart, swap-even, and vanish on the collision locus. Under those weaker assumptions, the defects are automatically \(O((h_1-h_2)^2)\), and the already-established good-patch defect-closeness mechanism excludes the branch. 

## 3. The quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model now has a sharp global reduction

The updated fixed-shear side is no longer just “some quotient transport model.” It now has a specific global compression in terms of sign-runs and lobe amplitudes \(B_j\). The live data are the run amplitudes, not the raw zero list. The exact distance-two overlap criterion is the three-lobe inequality
\[
B_j+B_{j+2}>B_{j+1}.
\]
Two consecutive distance-two separations are impossible. Therefore no-reentry on a quotient-visible branch forces at most three sign-runs, and in the three-run case the only surviving condition is
\[
B_1\ge B_0+B_2,
\]
with strict inequality giving the genuinely live case and equality giving only sterile touching. The quartic–sextic rung is explicitly inert for this lobe-amplitude obstruction because \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\) there. This is a real global reduction inside the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model. 

My attack then pushed that strict three-run case further. The natural inverse-branch object is
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
where \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) are the outer inverse branches over the common value interval. Strict excess is exactly the existence of an interior critical point of \(\Delta\). That is already a major simplification: the live fixed-shear lane is now an exact one-variable inverse-branch-gap problem. 

From there I derived a sequence of sharper reformulations.

First, if \(q_*\) is an interior critical point of \(\Delta\), then
\[
\phi_0'(q_*)=\phi_2'(q_*),
\]
hence the corresponding outer-run points \(u_0,u_2\) satisfy
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\qquad
\widehat Q'(u_0)=\widehat Q'(u_2).
\]
So strict excess forces a **same-value same-slope coincidence** across the separated outer runs. That turns the strict-excess fork into a phase-curve self-intersection problem for
\[
\Psi(u)=(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u)).
\]
This was one of my main new reductions beyond the consolidated update. It is reasoning from the branch-gap formulas, not something already proved in the draft. Its premises come from the quotient-visible inverse-branch model in the summary. 

Second, if we define
\[
R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))},
\]
then
\[
\Delta'(q)=0\iff R(q)=1.
\]
So the strict-excess fork is equivalently the existence of an interior zero of \(R-1\). This gave a cleaner scalar target than the original geometric phrasing. Again, this is my downstream attack from the inverse-branch formulation in the summary. 

Third, letting
\[
F(u):=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
the inverse branches satisfy the same scalar ODE
\[
\phi_i'(q)=F(\phi_i(q)),
\]
and the gap satisfies
\[
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)).
\]
By the mean-value theorem there exists \(\xi(q)\) between the two outer inverse branches with
\[
\Delta'(q)=F'(\xi(q))\,\Delta(q)
=-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(\xi(q))}{(\widehat Q'(\xi(q)))^2}\,\Delta(q).
\]
So the sign of \(\Delta'\) is controlled by the sign of
\[
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2}
\]
somewhere on the same-value chord joining the two outer runs. This was another new attack reduction: the strict-excess fork becomes a sign/comparison problem for a single scalar field \(H\) on the outer-run transport strip, or more sharply on the family of same-value chords. This is my own reasoning layered on top of the quotient-visible inverse-branch model. 

Fourth, I derived the Green-kernel identity
\[
\Delta(q)-\ell_\Delta(q)
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,\Delta''(s)\,ds
=
\int_{q_-}^{q_+} G(q,s)\,\bigl(\Xi(\phi_0(s))-\Xi(\phi_2(s))\bigr)\,ds,
\]
with
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
This shows that the deviation of the outer branch-gap from its endpoint chord is controlled exactly by inverse-curvature mismatch across the outer runs. From there, I proposed the next theorem target: represent the three-lobe defect
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\]
as a positive weighted functional of \(\Delta-\ell_\Delta\), equivalently of the inverse-curvature mismatch. If that lands, strict excess becomes a weighted inverse-curvature imbalance problem. This is not in the summaries; it is the main new analytic attack I added on top of them. 

So on the exact fixed-shear side, the new progress since the latest paper update is: the live strict-excess case has been reduced further from a generic transport problem to a very specific family of one-variable targets:
- same-value same-slope exclusion on the outer runs,
- or one-crossing / monotonicity of \(R(q)-1\),
- or a sign theorem for \(H(u)=\widehat Q''/\widehat Q'^2\) on the outer-run transport strip or same-value chords,
- or a weighted-\(\Lambda\) formula for the branch-gap convexity defect.

These are not proved yet, but the attack now has a concrete scalar shape it did not have before.

## 4. The nonmixed/global affine lane is now reduced but not closed

On the nonmixed/global affine 4-point side, the derivative-geometry work produced both a real theorem-level compression and a hard ceiling.

The compression is: fixed-slope tangency/incidence is now a finite remote endpoint/branch incidence problem. Support curves are strictly concave pseudolines with single-crossing and betweenness; each ordered pair has at most one overlap window; endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\) are strictly monotone, so each endpoint/branch pair contributes at most one remote tangent incidence. Thus the one-pair global burden is no longer “arbitrary recurrence.” It is the existence of zeros of explicit monotone remote endpoint-gap functions. That is a real improvement. 

The ceiling is equally important: intrinsic affine/pseudoline geometry is universal for analytic plane curves. So pure planar / pseudoline / interval-graph / local-convexity arguments cannot close this lane by themselves. Any final contradiction there must use RH-specific source structure, not more naked affine geometry. This is a genuine no-go, not just a tactical preference. 

My own attack on this side therefore stopped trying to squeeze more pure geometry out of the lane. I now regard the correct target as: identify an RH-specific or source-coupled datum that constrains the zeros of the endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\), and then prove a one-zero impossibility or incompatibility theorem for those endpoint-gap zeros. In other words, derivative geometry has already taken the affine lane to its universal ceiling; what remains there is no longer geometric but source-coupled.

## 5. The honest order-7 / package lane is now much clearer

The corrected target on the package side is now explicit. The real order-7 datum is the quotient-septic class
\[
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}],
\]
not raw septic equality. Once two corrected germs agree through cubic and quintic order and differ only by odd reparametrization plus already-quotiented lower scalar/value gauge, the remaining order-7 mismatch is automatically a multiple of \(A_5^{\mathfrak f}\), so the quotient-septic defect vanishes. Therefore the honest theorem target is a package-level statement such as same reduced image germ at coincidence or collision-functoriality of the corrected block triple on the collision/cancellation locus. If that holds, the quotient-septic collapse is formal afterward. This is now the clean global/package-level lane. turn65file1

The generator audit is central here. The consolidated update says no second primitive pointwise highest-new generator has appeared beyond \(r=q^{(7)}\), and all the currently explicit pointwise fields remain in the differential algebra generated by the lower field and \(r\). So if a package-level obstruction survives, it should be transport/global/provenance-sensitive, not another local scalar field. 

My attack then tried to connect this package lane to the local good-patch lane.

On a good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},\qquad
M:=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c'A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
the one-pair tangent model pair \((C,K)\) has a unique cubic-killing section \(\theta_*(m)\), and the quintic value there is
\[
\Gamma(m)=K(m,\theta_*(m))
=
-\frac{M(m)}{\sqrt{c(m)^2+c'(m)^2}}
\neq 0.
\]
So any local same-order compensator must change the **section-corrected quintic gap** after recentering the cubic-zero section. For an infinitesimal deformation pair \((U,V)\), that detector is
\[
\delta\Gamma_{(U,V)}
=
V(m,\theta_*(m))
-
\frac{\partial_\theta K(m,\theta_*(m))}{\partial_\theta C(m,\theta_*(m))}
\,U(m,\theta_*(m)).
\]
A same-order local compensator is exactly a deformation with
\[
\delta\Gamma=-\Gamma\neq 0.
\]

This led to my sharp bridge conjecture:

- odd reparametrization and lower scalar/value gauge should satisfy \(\delta\Gamma=0\);
- therefore any deformation with \(\delta\Gamma\neq 0\) is nontrivial in the reduced deformation quotient;
- and the first nontrivial transverse class should already be the quotient-septic class.

Equivalently, a local good-patch compensator should already be **quotient-septic-visible**. The useful contrapositive is:
\[
[A_7^{\mathfrak f}]=0
\Longrightarrow
\text{no same-order local compensator on any good patch.}
\]

That is not yet proved, but it is the cleanest bridge I found between the local good-patch lane and the global package lane.

Then I sharpened this further inside the explicit pointwise package. If the no-second-generator audit really implies that the first explicit pointwise transverse quotient is at most one-dimensional and generated by the \(r\)-direction, then there is a complete dichotomy:

- either the \(r\)-direction is **gap-visible**, meaning \(\delta\Gamma_r\neq 0\), in which case inside the explicit pointwise package
  \[
  \text{local good-patch compensator}
  \Longleftrightarrow
  [A_7^{\mathfrak f}]\neq 0;
  \]
- or the \(r\)-direction is **gap-invisible**, meaning \(\delta\Gamma_r=0\), in which case the whole explicit pointwise package is invisible to the local good-patch detector and any surviving local obstruction must already lie outside that explicit package, i.e. in nonlocal transport, collision-functoriality failure, or genuinely provenance-sensitive two-atom data.

This is one of the sharpest new endgame forks I arrived at. It is not in the paper update itself; it is my inference from combining the generator audit with the section-corrected gap detector. Its premises come from the generator audit and the good-patch tangent model. turn65file1

## 6. Strong no-gos and ceilings that now look permanent

A number of negative findings are now strong enough that I would treat them as permanent ceilings unless new source-coupled structure is injected.

The naive source-summed corrected-block route cannot produce the needed linear merger law. 

The exact fixed-shear corner cannot be saved by a second local transport variable, a third finite-order reset, or a two-pair model built only from separate descended one-atom states with natural merger law.

Those are real ceilings, not just failed attempts.

## 7. The best current attack map

So where does the attack now stand?

- one-crossing / monotonicity for the slope ratio \(R(q)\);

- or a representation of the three-lobe defect \(\Lambda=B_1-(B_0+B_2)\) as a positive weighted functional of \(\Delta-\ell_\Delta\), reducing strict excess to a weighted inverse-curvature mismatch problem.

These are now genuinely one-variable statements inside the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model. They are the sharpest new mathematical targets I produced since the most recent paper update. Their premises come from the consolidated fixed-shear reduction. 

On the nonmixed/global affine side, the right next attack is no longer more naked affine geometry. It is RH-specific exclusion of zeros of the monotone remote endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\). That is the only live burden left there. 

On the package/order-7 side, the right next attack is same reduced image germ or collision-functoriality of the corrected block triple. The most ambitious conjectural bridge is that on a good patch the local good-patch compensator detector \(\delta\Gamma\) and the quotient-septic detector are actually the same first transverse direction, at least inside the explicit pointwise package. turn65file1

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:00:36)

Here is the next honest continuation of the attack.

### 1. **Focus on Proving that \( R(q)-1 \) Cannot Vanish in the Interior in the Strict-Excess Case**

As already stated, strict excess is equivalent to the existence of an interior zero of \( R(q)-1 \), where
\[
R(q) = \frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
\]
The goal is to prove that \( R(q)-1 \) cannot vanish at any point in the interior of the common value interval \( I = [q_-, q_+] \). This will collapse the strict-excess condition, as we’ve already reduced the problem to proving that no interior zero of \( R(q)-1 \) exists in the strict case.

### Reformulate \( R(q)-1 \):

We know that
\[
R(q) = \frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
\]
Thus, \( R(q)-1 = 0 \) would imply that
\[
\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q)) = \widehat Q'(\phi_2(q)),
\]
which is exactly the condition for \( R(q)-1 = 0 \). In the strict-excess case, we are interested in showing that this condition cannot be satisfied at any interior point of \( I \), given that \( B_1 > B_0 + B_2 \).

### 2. **Key Strategy: Using \(\Xi(u) = \frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}\)**

From earlier work, we know that the gap function \(\Delta(q) = \phi_2(q) - \phi_0(q)\) satisfies:
\[
\Delta'(q) = \frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))} - \frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]
Strict excess is equivalent to \(\Delta'(q) = 0\) at some point in \( I \), which means that
\[
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))} = \frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]
This leads directly to the condition \( R(q) = 1 \), which is the critical point for the strict-excess case. 

### Sign of \(\Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\):

The key to attacking this is using \(\Xi(u) = \frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}\) to examine the curvature mismatch between \(\phi_0(q)\) and \(\phi_2(q)\). If \(\Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\) has a fixed sign on \( I \), then \(\Delta'(q)\) cannot change sign and, hence, cannot have an interior zero.

The function \(\Xi(u)\) is the key to understanding the behavior of \( R(q) \). By showing that \(\Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\) has a fixed sign (i.e., it is either always positive or always negative), we can guarantee that \(\Delta'(q)\) remains one-sided, which prevents an interior zero of \( R(q)-1 \).

### 3. **Monotonicity of \( R(q) \):**

We know from earlier work that the ratio \( R(q) \) is linked to the inverse of \(\widehat Q'(u)\). By proving that \(\Xi(u)\) has a fixed sign on the common value interval, we would establish that the outer inverse branches \(\phi_0\) and \(\phi_2\) evolve monotonically with respect to the value of \( q \). This means that \( R(q) \) would be either strictly greater than 1 or strictly less than 1, but it would not cross 1. This monotonicity is a direct consequence of the fixed sign of the curvature mismatch \(\Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\).

### 4. **Concrete Theorem Target for the Strict-Excess Fork:**

Given the above reasoning, the next concrete theorem to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{In the strict three-run quotient-visible model, } R(q)-1 \text{ cannot vanish in the interior.}
}
\]

This will **kill the strict-excess condition** by showing that \( R(q) \) cannot hit 1 in the interior. The proof will rely on the fixed sign of \(\Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\) and the fact that \(\Delta'(q)\) is controlled by the inverse-curvature mismatch. This would be a direct consequence of showing that the curvature mismatch \(\Xi(\phi_0) - \Xi(\phi_2)\) does not change sign in the interior of \( I \).

---

### 5. **Subsequent Steps:**

After this, the next logical step would be to:

1. **Prove that \(\Xi(\phi_0) - \Xi(\phi_2)\) has a fixed sign on \( I \),** which will ensure that \(\Delta'(q)\) remains monotone and does not change sign.
2. **Verify the monotonicity of \( R(q) \)** using this fixed-sign property of \(\Xi(\phi_0) - \Xi(\phi_2)\).
3. **Prove that \( R(q) \) cannot hit 1 in the interior** in the strict-excess case, thus killing the strict-excess condition altogether.

These steps will finalize the attack on the strict-excess fixed-shear fork, closing that part of the proof. The rest of the work would then focus on proving the **same reduced image germ** or **collision-functoriality** at the package level. But for now, we focus entirely on this one-variable problem in the strict-excess case.

### 1. **Proving that \( R(q) - 1 \) Cannot Vanish in the Interior (Revisited)**

In order to kill the strict-excess condition, we must prove that \( R(q) - 1 \) **cannot vanish in the interior** of the common value interval \( I = [q_-, q_+] \), as we have already reduced the problem to proving this impossibility under the condition that \( B_1 > B_0 + B_2 \).

### 3. **Monotonicity of \( R(q) \)**

The next step is to establish **monotonicity** for \( R(q) \). Since \(\Delta'(q)\) depends on the difference between the curvature mismatches \(\Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\), we need to show that this difference does not change sign in the interior of the common value interval.

- If \(\Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\) has a fixed sign, then \(\Delta'(q)\) is monotonic.
- A **monotonic function** can only cross a fixed value (like 1) once, so this would prevent \( R(q) \) from hitting 1 more than once in the interval.

### 4. **Proving that \( R(q) - 1 \) Cannot Vanish in the Interior:**

Given that \(\Xi(\phi_0(q)) - \Xi(\phi_2(q))\) has fixed sign, we now conclude that \( \Delta'(q) \) is monotonic. Therefore, \( R(q) \), which is defined in terms of \(\Delta'(q)\), is also monotonic. 

Because \( R(q) \) is monotonic, it can cross 1 **at most once**. This means that an interior zero of \( R(q) - 1 \) is impossible, as it would require \( R(q) \) to cross 1 twice, which contradicts monotonicity.

### 5. **Final Theorem for Strict Excess:**

Recall that:
\[
\Xi(u) = \frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.

_Thought for 35s_

Here is the next real push. I can sharpen the strict-excess fixed-shear attack one more time.

The important improvement is this: I should stop talking about the sign of \(H(u)=\widehat Q''(u)/(\widehat Q'(u))^2\) at an unspecified mean-value point, and instead use the **exact secant-slope identity** for the outer inverse branches. That gives a cleaner one-variable target.

We are on the strict three-run quotient-visible lane, where the live case is
\[
B_1>B_0+B_2,
\]
and the outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) over the common value interval \(I\) define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
The consolidated notes already identify this inverse-branch-gap problem as the right endgame on that lane.【225†source】【226†source】

Now set
\[
F(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]
Then each outer inverse branch satisfies
\[
\phi_i'(q)=F(\phi_i(q)).
\]
So the gap satisfies the **exact** identity
\[
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)).
\]
Since \(\Delta(q)>0\) on the overlap interval, divide by \(\Delta(q)\) and get
\[
\frac{\Delta'(q)}{\Delta(q)}
=
\frac{F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q))}{\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)}.
\]
The right-hand side is the secant slope of \(F\) across the same-value chord joining the two outer runs.

This is much better than the earlier mean-value formulation, because it is exact. Writing
\[
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2},
\qquad
F'(u)=-H(u),
\]
we get the exact averaged form
\[
\frac{\Delta'(q)}{\Delta(q)}
=
\frac{1}{\Delta(q)}\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} F'(u)\,du
=
-\frac{1}{\Delta(q)}\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du.
\]

So the strict-excess fork has now become:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess } \Longleftrightarrow \exists q_*\in I \text{ such that }
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H(u)\,du=0.
}
\]

That is the sharpest scalar reformulation I have so far.

It means I do **not** need a pointwise sign theorem for \(H\) on the whole strip. I only need a theorem about the **same-value chord averages** of \(H\). That is a significantly more natural target, because the quotient-visible three-run model is already organized by same-value transport between the outer runs.【225†source】【226†source】

So the next theorem target should be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Same-value chord-average sign theorem.}
}
\]

A good form is:

> In the strict three-run quotient-visible model \(B_1>B_0+B_2\), the chord average
> \[
> \mathcal A(q):=\frac{1}{\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)}
> \int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du
> \]
> keeps a strict sign on the common value interval \(I\).

If that holds, then
\[
\frac{\Delta'}{\Delta}=-\mathcal A(q)
\]
keeps a strict sign, so \(\Delta'\) keeps a strict sign, so \(\Delta\) has no interior critical point, and strict excess is impossible.

This is stronger than the earlier “\(R(q)-1\) cannot vanish” target because it identifies the exact scalar obstruction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess can survive only if the same-value chord-average of }H
\text{ vanishes somewhere.}
}
\]

There is also a very useful equivalent formulation in terms of the slope ratio
\[
R(q):=\frac{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}.
\]
Since
\[
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)),
\]
we have
\[
\Delta'(q)=0 \iff R(q)=1.
\]
But now we can sharpen that to
\[
\operatorname{sgn}(R(q)-1)
=
-\operatorname{sgn}\!\left(\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du\right),
\]
because \(\Delta(q)>0\). So the problem is no longer “prove \(R\) monotone” in the abstract. It is “prove the same-value chord-average of \(H\) never vanishes in the strict case.”

That suggests the next two concrete sub-attacks.

First, try to connect the three-lobe defect
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
\]
directly to this chord-average quantity. The dream statement is:

\[
\boxed{
\Lambda>0
\Longrightarrow
\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du
\text{ has fixed strict sign for all }q\in I.
}
\]

That would kill the strict-excess fork immediately.

Second, if that is too hard globally, try for the weaker but still decisive statement:

\[
\boxed{
\int_{\phi_0(q_*)}^{\phi_2(q_*)} H(u)\,du=0
\Longrightarrow
B_1=B_0+B_2.
}
\]

That is exactly the “same-value same-slope implies equality-case touching” theorem, but now phrased in the cleanest integral form.

This is a real sharpening over my earlier attack. Before, I was asking for pointwise sign control of \(H\) or \(\Xi\). Now the exact obstruction is the **average of \(H\) along same-value chords**, which is much closer to the actual transport geometry.

So the next specific way I should continue is:

1. Work with the exact identity
   \[
   \frac{\Delta'}{\Delta}
   =
   -\frac{1}{\Delta}\int_{\phi_0}^{\phi_2} H(u)\,du.
   \]

2. Try to express the lobe-amplitude defect
   \[
   \Lambda=B_1-(B_0+B_2)
   \]
   as a positive weighted functional of either \(\Delta-\ell_\Delta\) or directly of the chord-average of \(H\). The consolidated notes make clear that \(\Lambda\) is the only genuinely live scalar defect left on the strict three-run model, so it is exactly the right quantity to couple to this average.【225†source】【226†source】

3. Prove either:
   - strict \(\Lambda>0\) forces the chord-average of \(H\) to keep one sign, or
   - any zero of that chord-average forces \(\Lambda=0\).

That is now the sharpest and most specific math continuation I see on the fixed-shear front.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:18:10)

_Thought for 8s_

Here is the next clean push.

I think the best continuation now is to make the strict three-run fixed-shear lane into a **Volterra-type sign problem** for the branch-gap, instead of treating it only as a pointwise critical-point question.

We already have the exact identity
\[
\frac{\Delta'(q)}{\Delta(q)}
=
-\frac{1}{\Delta(q)}\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du,
\qquad
H(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2},
\]
with
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0.
\]
So the strict-excess fork is exactly the possibility that the same-value chord average of \(H\) vanishes somewhere. That is sharper than the earlier “maybe \(R(q)=1\)” language, because it identifies the precise scalar obstruction.

The next thing I would now attack is to differentiate this identity once more and see whether the chord-average can satisfy a closed differential inequality. Define
\[
A(q):=\frac{1}{\Delta(q)}\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)} H(u)\,du,
\]
so
\[
\Delta'(q)=-A(q)\Delta(q).
\]
Then
\[
\Delta''(q)=-(A'(q)+A(q)^2)\Delta(q).
\]
But independently,
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So we get the exact relation
\[
\boxed{
A'(q)+A(q)^2
=
-\frac{\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))}{\Delta(q)}.
}
\]

This is a very useful reformulation. It says the strict-excess fork is now a Riccati-type evolution problem for the chord-average \(A(q)\). In particular, a zero of \(A\) is not free: if \(A(q_*)=0\), then
\[
A'(q_*)=
-\frac{\Xi(\phi_0(q_*))-\Xi(\phi_2(q_*))}{\Delta(q_*)}.
\]
So at any same-value/same-slope witness, the sign of \(A'\) is determined entirely by the inverse-curvature mismatch across the two outer branches.

That gives a much sharper next theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{At any zero of the chord-average }A,\textbf{ the derivative }A'
\textbf{ has the sign forcing only sterile touching, not strict excess.}
}
\]

In other words, the next thing to prove is not yet global sign of \(A\), but a **zero-crossing exclusion law**:
if \(A(q_*)=0\), then the local behavior of \(A\) near \(q_*\) is incompatible with the strict three-run excess regime \(B_1>B_0+B_2\).

Why this is attractive is that it reduces the problem to a very local condition at a hypothetical witness \(q_*\). We no longer need a full sign theorem for \(H\) or \(\Xi\) on the whole strip right away. We only need enough control to show that when the same-value chord average vanishes, the induced curvature mismatch pushes the system into the equality/touching side rather than the strict side. That fits the consolidated picture very well: equality is sterile touching, strict excess is the only genuinely live case.【225†source】【226†source】

So the next nested attack stack on this lane is:

First, suppose
\[
A(q_*)=0.
\]
Then
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
so we are at a same-value/same-slope witness.

Second, use
\[
A'(q_*)=
-\frac{\Xi(\phi_0(q_*))-\Xi(\phi_2(q_*))}{\Delta(q_*)}
\]
to classify the crossing. If one can prove that the right-hand side either vanishes or has only the sign compatible with equality-case touching, then strict excess is dead.

Third, if possible, go one order higher. Differentiating again gives an expression for \(A''(q_*)\) in terms of higher \(\Xi\)-jet mismatch, so there is a natural even-jet ladder here. This matches the recent note that the strict three-run lane has an “even-jet mismatch witness” in the reciprocal-slope tower. The Riccati formulation makes that concrete: if the first nonzero derivative of \(A\) at a zero must be of even order, then the witness is touching; if odd order is impossible, strict excess is gone.【226†source】

So the specific theorem I would now try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Any zero of the same-value chord-average }A(q)
\textbf{ on the strict three-run quotient-visible model is an even-order touching zero.}
}
\]

That would be enough, because even-order touching belongs to the sterile boundary \(B_1=B_0+B_2\), not to strict excess.

Equivalently, in the original inverse-branch language:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{A same-value/same-slope coincidence across the outer runs cannot produce a genuine sign change of }\Delta'.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest next theorem I see on the fixed-shear front, and it is more specific than my previous formulations. It uses the new exact Riccati identity for the chord-average, which I think is the right analytic object to push now.
