## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:19:04)

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next push.

The Riccati reformulation suggests a more precise local target than I had before: instead of trying to rule out zeros of the chord-average
\[
A(q):=\frac{1}{\Delta(q)}\int_{\phi_0(q)}^{\phi_2(q)}H(u)\,du,
\qquad
H(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^2},
\]
globally, I should now attack the **first nonzero jet of \(A\) at a hypothetical zero**.

We have the exact identities
\[
\Delta'(q)=-A(q)\Delta(q),
\]
and
\[
A'(q)+A(q)^2
=
-\frac{\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))}{\Delta(q)},
\qquad
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}.
\]
So if \(q_*\) is a hypothetical strict-excess witness, then
\[
A(q_*)=0,
\qquad
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
and therefore
\[
A'(q_*)
=
-\frac{\Xi(\phi_0(q_*))-\Xi(\phi_2(q_*))}{\Delta(q_*)}.
\]

That already gives the first bifurcation:

If \(A'(q_*)\neq 0\), then \(A\) changes sign linearly, so \(\Delta'\) changes sign linearly, and \(q_*\) is a genuine odd-order interior extremum of \(\Delta\). That is the true strict-excess case.

If \(A'(q_*)=0\), then the witness is at least one order more degenerate, i.e. touching-like. That is much closer to the sterile boundary. So the next theorem target should be:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{At any same-value / same-slope outer witness, }
\Xi(\phi_0(q_*))=\Xi(\phi_2(q_*)).
}
\]

This is exactly the equal-inverse-curvature statement I had been circling, but now it arises as the clean condition
\[
A'(q_*)=0.
\]
If I can prove that, then every zero of \(A\) is at least a double zero. That already kills the simplest strict-excess scenario. 【225†source】【226†source】

Then the next step is to differentiate once more. Since \(A(q_*)=A'(q_*)=0\), differentiating the Riccati identity gives
\[
A''(q_*)
=
-\frac{d}{dq}\left(\frac{\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))}{\Delta(q)}\right)\Bigg|_{q=q_*}.
\]
So the second derivative of \(A\) is governed by the first transported jet-mismatch of \(\Xi\) across the two outer branches. This is where the “reciprocal-slope tower” from the update note starts to become concrete: if the first nonzero mismatch in that tower is forced to occur in even order, then \(A\) has only even-order zeros, hence \(\Delta'\) has only touching zeros, not genuine strict-excess sign changes. 【226†source】

That suggests the sharper theorem ladder:

First target:
\[
\boxed{
\text{same-value/same-slope} \Longrightarrow \Xi(\phi_0)=\Xi(\phi_2).
}
\]

Second target:
\[
\boxed{
\text{same-value/same-slope/equal-}\Xi
\Longrightarrow
\text{the first nonzero reciprocal-slope jet mismatch is even-order.}
}
\]

Third conclusion:
\[
\boxed{
\text{Any zero of }A\text{ is even-order, hence belongs only to the sterile touching regime.}
}
\]

That would kill strict excess.

There is also a slightly weaker but maybe more attackable version: instead of proving full equality of \(\Xi\) at same-value/same-slope witnesses, try to prove only the sign statement
\[
A'(q_*) \text{ cannot have the sign needed for a strict local minimum of }\Delta.
\]
That would already exclude the live branch. Concretely, since
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=-A'(q_*)\Delta(q_*),
\]
and \(\Delta(q_*)>0\), the sign of \(\Delta''(q_*)\) is exactly the negative sign of \(A'(q_*)\). So one can target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{At any same-value/same-slope outer witness, }\Delta''(q_*)\le 0
\textbf{ (or }\ge 0\textbf{), with only the touching sign possible.}
}
\]

That would already rule out a genuine strict local minimum of \(\Delta\).

So the specific way I should continue now is:

1. Treat a hypothetical strict-excess witness as a zero of \(A\).
2. Use the exact formula
   \[
   A'(q_*)=
   -\frac{\Xi(\phi_0(q_*))-\Xi(\phi_2(q_*))}{\Delta(q_*)}
   \]
   to reduce the problem to equality or sign control of transported \(\Xi\).
3. If equality looks reachable, aim for \(A'(q_*)=0\).
4. If not, aim for the weaker one-sided sign law on \(A'(q_*)\) that forbids a strict local minimum of \(\Delta\).
5. Then, if needed, push to \(A''\) and the reciprocal-slope jet tower.

So the sharpest next theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{A same-value / same-slope outer witness cannot produce the first-order sign needed for strict excess.}
}
\]

That is more precise than my earlier “prove \(R(q)-1\) has no zero” target, and it is exactly where the new draft notes point: the strict three-run lane has become an even-jet / transport-witness problem, not a loose geometric one. 【226†source】

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:20:40)

_Thought for 5s_

Here is the next honest push.

I think the attack should now pivot from the first derivative of the chord-average \(A\) to an **intrinsic parity statement for the witness jet**, because that is exactly what the latest note is hinting at with the reciprocal-slope tower.【226†source】

Let me organize it cleanly.

We are on the strict three-run quotient-visible fixed-shear lane. Over the common value interval \(I\) of the two outer runs, define the outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2,
\qquad
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0,
\]
and the reciprocal-slope field
\[
J_1(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]
Then
\[
\phi_i'(q)=J_1(\phi_i(q)),
\]
so
\[
\Delta'(q)=J_1(\phi_2(q))-J_1(\phi_0(q)).
\]

A strict-excess witness is an interior point \(q_*\in I\) with
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
equivalently
\[
J_1(\phi_2(q_*))=J_1(\phi_0(q_*)).
\]
Since also
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=q_*,
\]
this is exactly a same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness across the two outer runs.【225†source】【226†source】

Now the right next move is to define the full transported mismatch tower
\[
\delta J_n(q):=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u):=J_1(u)\,\partial_u J_n(u).
\]
This is the clean value-space transport tower the note is pointing to.【226†source】

The reason this is the right object is that it differentiates perfectly along the inverse branches. Indeed,
\[
\frac{d}{dq}J_n(\phi_i(q))
=
J_n'(\phi_i(q))\,\phi_i'(q)
=
J_n'(\phi_i(q))\,J_1(\phi_i(q))
=
J_{n+1}(\phi_i(q)).
\]
So the mismatches satisfy the exact recursion
\[
\boxed{
\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q).
}
\]
That means the Taylor expansion of the witness function
\[
\delta J_1(q)=\Delta'(q)
\]
at \(q_*\) is controlled term-by-term by the transported tower:
\[
\delta J_1(q)
=
\sum_{m\ge 1}\frac{\delta J_{m+1}(q_*)}{m!}(q-q_*)^m.
\]

This is a major sharpening, because it says:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Strict excess is equivalent to saying that the first nonzero }\delta J_n(q_*)\text{ occurs at odd transport order.}
}
\]

Why? Because if the first nonzero derivative of \(\Delta'=\delta J_1\) at \(q_*\) has odd order, then \(\Delta'\) changes sign and \(q_*\) is a genuine extremum of \(\Delta\); if the first nonzero derivative has even order, then it is only touching. So the live strict-excess fork has become a **parity problem in the transported mismatch tower**.

That gives the clean next theorem target:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Parity theorem for outer transported mismatches: }
\text{at any same-value/same-}J_1\text{ witness, the first nonzero }\delta J_n
\text{ is even-order.}
}
\]

If that holds, then every interior zero of \(\Delta'\) is a touching zero, not a strict-excess zero. That would kill the strict case immediately.

This is better than my earlier “prove \(\Xi\)-equality” target, because it does not insist that the first mismatch vanishes at the \(J_2\) or \(\Xi\) level. It only asks for the weaker, and probably more natural, parity statement:
the first mismatch cannot occur in odd transport order.

There are now three progressively weaker sub-targets.

First, strongest:
\[
\boxed{
\delta J_2(q_*)=0.
}
\]
Since
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=\delta J_1'(q_*)=\Delta''(q_*),
\]
this says a same-value/same-slope witness cannot already be a strict local minimum/maximum of \(\Delta\). This is the equal-inverse-curvature statement in disguised form.

Second:
\[
\boxed{
\delta J_2(q_*)=\delta J_3(q_*)=\cdots=\delta J_{2k}(q_*)=0,\quad
\delta J_{2k+1}(q_*)\neq 0.
}
\]
This would mean the first nonzero mismatch occurs in even order for \(\Delta'\), hence only touching.

Third, weakest but still enough:
\[
\boxed{
\text{The first nonzero }\delta J_n(q_*)\text{ cannot occur at odd }n.
}
\]

That weakest parity statement is now, in my view, the sharpest next attack.

Why this is plausible from the current draft picture is that the strict three-run lane has already been compressed to a transport problem, and the latest note explicitly says the strengthened expected witness is an **even-jet mismatch witness in the reciprocal-slope tower**.【226†source】 That is almost exactly the conjecture above, just rewritten as a theorem target.

There is also a nice way to connect this back to the lobe defect
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2).
\]
The equality case \(\Lambda=0\) is already identified as touching, while strict \(\Lambda>0\) is the only live case.【225†source】【226†source】 So if one proves that any same-value/same-\(J_1\) witness forces even-order contact in the \(\delta J_n\)-tower, then such a witness belongs automatically to the equality/touching side, not the strict side. In other words:

\[
\boxed{
\text{same-value/same-slope witness}
\Longrightarrow
\Lambda=0,
}
\]
provided the parity theorem for the transported mismatch tower holds.

So the specific way I should continue now is:

1. Work at a hypothetical witness \(q_*\) with
   \[
   \delta J_1(q_*)=0.
   \]
2. Use
   \[
   \delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q)
   \]
   to identify the first nonzero Taylor coefficient of \(\Delta'=\delta J_1\).
3. Try to prove that this first nonzero coefficient cannot come from odd transport order.
4. The first concrete checkpoint is to prove
   \[
   \delta J_2(q_*)=0,
   \]
   i.e. equal inverse curvature across the witness.
5. If that is too hard, weaken to the parity theorem directly.

So the sharpest next theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{At any outer same-value / same-slope witness, the reciprocal-slope mismatch tower has even first nonzero jet.}
}
\]

That is the best next continuation I see on the strict three-run fixed-shear front.

Here is the next honest push.

I think the right continuation now is to turn the reciprocal-slope tower into an **exact local normal form at a hypothetical witness** and then ask what parity of the first nonzero term is actually compatible with the three-run geometry.

We are at an interior same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness \(q_*\), meaning
\[
\delta J_1(q_*):=J_1(\phi_2(q_*)) - J_1(\phi_0(q_*))=0,
\qquad
J_1(u)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]
Since
\[
\Delta'(q)=\delta J_1(q),
\]
this is exactly the strict-excess witness candidate. The new useful fact is the exact transport recursion
\[
\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q),
\qquad
J_{n+1}(u)=J_1(u)\,\partial_u J_n(u).
\]
So the whole Taylor series of \(\Delta'=\delta J_1\) at \(q_*\) is literally the transported mismatch tower:
\[
\delta J_1(q)
=
\sum_{m\ge 1}\frac{\delta J_{m+1}(q_*)}{m!}(q-q_*)^m.
\]
That means the local type of the witness is completely determined by the first nonzero \(\delta J_n(q_*)\).【226†source】

So the strict three-run fork has now become:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Can the first nonzero transported mismatch at a same-value/same-}J_1\text{ witness occur at odd order?}
}
\]

That is sharper than my earlier phrasing. It is no longer “maybe \(\Xi\) matches” or “maybe \(R\) is monotone.” It is a very concrete parity problem in the exact transported jet tower.

Now let
\[
m_*:=\min\{n\ge 2:\delta J_n(q_*)\neq 0\}.
\]
Then locally,
\[
\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{\delta J_{m_*}(q_*)}{(m_*-1)!}(q-q_*)^{m_*-1}
+ o\!\left((q-q_*)^{m_*-1}\right).
\]
So:

- if \(m_*-1\) is odd, then \(\Delta'\) changes sign and \(q_*\) is a genuine strict extremum of \(\Delta\);
- if \(m_*-1\) is even, then \(\Delta'\) only touches zero and there is no genuine sign change.

Equivalently:

\[
\boxed{
\text{strict excess requires }m_*\text{ to be even,}
}
\]
while
\[
\boxed{
\text{touching corresponds to }m_*\text{ odd.}
}
\]

This is the cleanest parity statement so far.

So the next theorem target should actually be one of these two mutually exclusive forms:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Odd-tower theorem: }
m_*\text{ is always odd at an outer same-value/same-}J_1\text{ witness.}
}
\]

or, weaker but still enough,

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Second-level vanishing: }
\delta J_2(q_*)=0.
}
\]

Because if \(\delta J_2(q_*)=0\), then the first possible nonzero mismatch is pushed to \(J_3\) or higher, and the witness is already no longer a first-order strict extremum. This is exactly the equal-inverse-curvature checkpoint in tower language. 【226†source】

Why this now looks like the right place to squeeze is that the latest note explicitly says the strengthened expected witness is an **even-jet mismatch witness in the reciprocal-slope tower**. In the notation above, that is precisely the claim that strict-extremum parity is wrong: the first nonzero mismatch should occur in the touching parity, not the strict parity. So the note is really pointing at the same theorem target. 【226†source】

There is also a helpful way to connect this to the lobe defect
\[
\Lambda:=B_1-(B_0+B_2).
\]
The updated draft treats
\[
\Lambda=0
\]
as sterile touching and
\[
\Lambda>0
\]
as the only genuinely live strict case. So if the tower parity theorem says every same-value/same-\(J_1\) witness has touching parity, then automatically:

\[
\boxed{
\text{same-value/same-slope witness} \Longrightarrow \Lambda=0,
}
\]
hence no such witness can occur in the strict case. That would kill the strict-excess fork exactly in the form the consolidated notes want. 【225†source】【226†source】

So the specific way I should continue now is this.

First, work at a hypothetical witness \(q_*\) and compute the first few tower terms explicitly:
\[
\delta J_1(q_*)=0,\qquad
\delta J_2(q_*)=\delta J_1'(q_*),\qquad
\delta J_3(q_*)=\delta J_2'(q_*),
\]
and so on.

Second, try to show \(\delta J_2(q_*)=0\). That is the first concrete checkpoint. In the earlier language, this is exactly equality of inverse curvature across the witness.

Third, if full vanishing of \(\delta J_2\) is too strong, try instead to prove the parity statement directly: the first nonzero \(\delta J_n(q_*)\) must occur with touching parity.

Fourth, once that parity statement is obtained, feed it back into the lobe-amplitude classification to conclude that any such witness belongs only to the equality/touching regime \(B_1=B_0+B_2\), never to strict excess.

So the sharpest next theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{At any outer same-value / same-}J_1\textbf{ witness, the transported mismatch tower has touching parity.}
}
\]

That is the most specific and most aligned continuation I can see from the latest update notes.【226†source】

\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q),

so the Taylor series of the witness function
\[

- if the first nonzero \(\delta J_n(q_*)\) occurs at **even** \(n\), then \(\Delta'\) changes sign and \(q_*\) is a genuine strict extremum;

\delta J_2(q_*)= -\bigl(\Xi(\phi_2(q_*))-\Xi(\phi_0(q_*))\bigr).

\boxed{
\delta J_2(q_*)=0
\quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad
\Xi(\phi_0(q_*))=\Xi(\phi_2(q_*)).
}
\]

That is exactly the equal-inverse-curvature statement, now in the right tower language. If this holds, then every witness is already at least one order more degenerate than a genuine strict extremum. This matches the consolidated transport endgame very closely: either \(\Xi\)-descent holds and the witness gets pushed into touching parity, or \(\Xi\)-descent fails and that failure is itself the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction.【225†source】【226†source】

\boxed{
\textbf{Fork 1: }\Xi\textbf{-descent holds across outer same-value pairs.}
}

Then \(\delta J_2=0\) at every witness, so the first possible nonzero mismatch is higher-order. The next job is to prove that the first surviving mismatch still has touching parity, ideally by bootstrapping the same descent idea up the \(J_n\)-tower.

\boxed{
\textbf{Fork 2: }\Xi\textbf{-descent fails.}

Then that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction. In that case the strict three-run lane is no longer a local scalar issue; it has become a genuine transport/package obstruction, exactly as the notes say.【226†source】

That is already a cleaner endgame than before, because it says the strict three-run lane cannot stay in a vague middle state. It either collapses into touching parity or escapes into explicit nonlocal transport.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:35:49)

_Thought for 19s_

Here is the next sharpened attack.

I think the right continuation now is to stop treating the transported mismatch tower as just a parity gadget and turn it into a **closure-law problem**.

The key point is this: at a hypothetical strict three-run witness \(q_*\), we already have a same-value / same-\(J_1\) pair on the two outer runs,
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*,
\qquad
J_1(\phi_0(q_*))=J_1(\phi_2(q_*)),
\qquad
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]
The notes are now clearly steering the fixed-shear lane toward exactly this sort of one-variable transport closure, including the candidate first-order closure law
\[
\widehat Q''(u)=G(\widehat Q(u),\widehat Q'(u)),
\]
equivalently a branch-independent law for \(J_2\) in terms of \((\widehat Q,J_1)\).【225†source】【226†source】

That suggests the clean next fork:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Either }J_2\textbf{ descends to value-space data }(\widehat Q,J_1),
\textbf{ or failure of that descent is the first genuine nonlocal transport obstruction.}
}
\]

This is sharper than my previous parity-only framing.

## 1. The exact tower setup

Define
\[
J_{n+1}(u):=J_1(u)\,\partial_u J_n(u),
\qquad
\delta J_n(q):=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]
Then along the outer inverse branches,
\[
\frac{d}{dq}J_n(\phi_i(q))=J_{n+1}(\phi_i(q)),
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q).
}
\]
Hence the Taylor series of the witness function
\[
\Delta'(q)=\delta J_1(q)
\]
at \(q_*\) is controlled term-by-term by the tower:
\[
\delta J_1(q)
=
\sum_{m\ge 1}\frac{\delta J_{m+1}(q_*)}{m!}(q-q_*)^m.
\]

So the local type of the witness is determined by the first nonzero transported mismatch. That much was already clear. The new question is: can one **close** the tower using only same-value data?【226†source】

## 2. The real first theorem target: first-order closure

The first concrete theorem I would now attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{First-order closure law: }
J_2(u)=\mathcal G\bigl(\widehat Q(u),J_1(u)\bigr)
\textbf{ on the relevant outer-shell transport region.}
}
\]

Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=
-\Xi(u),
\]
this is exactly the \(\Xi\)-descent statement in the right coordinates. If it holds, then at any same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness we automatically get
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0.
\]
So the first possible nonzero transported mismatch is pushed to \(J_3\) or higher.

This is cleaner than merely asking for \(\Xi(\phi_0)=\Xi(\phi_2)\) at a witness. It says that equality is forced because \(J_2\) is not an independent branchwise datum once \((\widehat Q,J_1)\) are fixed. That is exactly the kind of closure the updated note is pointing to.【226†source】

## 3. What happens if first-order closure holds

If
\[
J_2=\mathcal G(\widehat Q,J_1),
\]
then at a witness
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0.
\]

Now differentiate \(\delta J_1'(q)=\delta J_2(q)\). Since \(\delta J_2(q_*)=0\), the witness is at least one order more degenerate than a genuine strict extremum. But there is more: if one can continue the closure inductively, namely prove
\[
\boxed{
J_{n+1}(u)=\mathcal G_n\bigl(\widehat Q(u),J_1(u),\dots,J_n(u)\bigr),
}
\]
then same \((\widehat Q,J_1)\) at the witness forces same \(J_2\), hence same \(J_3\), and so on. In other words:

\[
\boxed{
\text{same-value / same-}J_1
\Longrightarrow
\delta J_n(q_*)=0\text{ for all }n.
}
\]

And once the whole transported tower agrees, the outer shells are no longer merely touching in one jet: they are formally identical as value-space transport germs. At that point the only plausible outcome is exact translated replication of the outer shells, which the update note already identifies as the descent-side endgame:
\[
\Omega_0=\Omega_2,\qquad B_0=B_2,\qquad \phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C,
\qquad
\widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)\text{ on }R_0.
\]
That belongs to the equality/touching side, not the strict-excess side.【226†source】

So if tower closure holds, strict excess dies.

## 4. What happens if first-order closure fails

If
\[
J_2 \neq \mathcal G(\widehat Q,J_1)
\]
on the transport region, then the failure of that descent is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction. This is exactly the other branch of the fixed-shear endgame the update note describes: either \(\Xi\)-descent holds and the shells rigidify, or \(\Xi\)-descent fails and that failure itself is the obstruction.【226†source】

So the lane now has a very sharp fork:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Fork A: first-order closure holds } \Rightarrow \text{ witness is pushed into full-shell rigidity / touching.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Fork B: first-order closure fails } \Rightarrow \text{ the first honest obstruction is already explicit and nonlocal.}
}
\]

That is a much cleaner endgame than “maybe parity works out.”

## 5. Why this is the right next theorem

Because it matches the updated draft’s new structure almost perfectly:

- the fixed-shear strict three-run lane is no longer “some transport issue” but an inverse-branch-gap / \(\Xi\)-transport fork;
- the close exact fixed-shear lane is already expected to reduce to scalar-local buds;
- the generator audit says the surviving burden should be transport/global/package-level, not one more primitive field.【225†source】【226†source】

So the next useful theorem is not another geometric contradiction. It is a **branch-independent closure theorem**.

## 6. The specific way I should continue

The next concrete attack is:

1. Try to prove the first-order closure law
   \[
   J_2(u)=\mathcal G(\widehat Q(u),J_1(u))
   \]
   on the outer-shell transport region.

2. If that seems too strong globally, weaken it to the witness version:
   \[
   \boxed{
   \text{same-value / same-}J_1 \Longrightarrow \text{same }J_2.
   }
   \]

3. If that lands, push one step up the tower:
   \[
   \text{same-value / same-}J_1 / \text{same }J_2
   \Longrightarrow \text{same }J_3,
   \]
   and so on.

4. If the first closure step fails, stop treating the strict three-run lane as a local jet-parity problem and promote that failure explicitly as the first nonlocal transport obstruction.

So the sharpest next theorem target is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{First-order transport closure on the outer-shell witness set: }
(\widehat Q,J_1)\text{ determines }J_2.
}
\]

If that lands, the whole parity tower starts to collapse into translated replication / touching, and the strict-excess fork is essentially finished. If it fails, the obstruction is already explicitly nonlocal, which is also a real advance.

I think the right continuation now is to make the “first-order closure” target maximally concrete by isolating the **exact compatibility condition** it would impose on the outer witness set.

We are on the strict three-run quotient-visible fixed-shear fork. At a hypothetical outer witness \(q_*\), we have a same-value / same-\(J_1\) pair
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*,
\qquad
J_1(\phi_0(q_*))=J_1(\phi_2(q_*)),
\qquad

\]
The recent update notes are clearly pushing this lane toward a branch-independent closure law for the reciprocal-slope tower, starting with \(J_2\).【225†source】【226†source】

So the next theorem target should be phrased as:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Witness-set closure: on the outer witness set, }(\widehat Q,J_1)\textbf{ determines }J_2.

That is weaker than a global formula
\[
J_2=\mathcal G(\widehat Q,J_1)
\]
everywhere, but it is exactly what the strict-excess fork needs.

## 1. Why this is the right weakening

The strict-excess obstruction only cares about points where the two outer runs have the same \(\widehat Q\)-value and the same \(J_1\)-value. So a full branch-independent closure theorem on the whole shell may be stronger than necessary. The genuinely minimal theorem is:

> whenever two outer-shell points satisfy
> \[
> (\widehat Q(u_0),J_1(u_0))=(\widehat Q(u_2),J_1(u_2)),
> \]
> then automatically
> \[
> J_2(u_0)=J_2(u_2).
> \]

That is exactly the statement
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0
\]
at every witness, and it is the cleanest way to formulate “\(\Xi\)-descent at the witness level.”

Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)=-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}=-\Xi(u),
\]
this is the same as saying:
\[
\boxed{
\text{same-value / same-}J_1 \Longrightarrow \text{same inverse curvature } \Xi.
}
\]

That is the exact first closure checkpoint.

## 2. What this buys immediately

Recall the transported mismatch tower
\[
\delta J_n(q):=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)),
\qquad
J_{n+1}=J_1\partial_u J_n,
\]
with exact recursion
\[
\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q).
\]
Since
\[
\Delta'(q)=\delta J_1(q),
\]
the Taylor series at a witness \(q_*\) is
\[
\delta J_1(q)=\sum_{m\ge1}\frac{\delta J_{m+1}(q_*)}{m!}(q-q_*)^m.
\]

So if witness-set closure gives
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,
\]
then the first possible nonzero transported mismatch is pushed to \(J_3\) or higher. In particular,
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=0.
\]
So a same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness cannot already be a first-order strict extremum of \(\Delta\). That is a real squeeze.

This is stronger than my earlier “maybe parity works out” language, because it identifies the exact first place where the strict-extremum mechanism must fail.

## 3. The next natural closure step

Once \(J_2\) is locked by \((\widehat Q,J_1)\) on the witness set, the next question is whether that propagates upward.

The natural inductive statement is:

\[
\boxed{
(\widehat Q,J_1,\dots,J_n)\text{ agree at two outer points}
\Longrightarrow
J_{n+1}\text{ also agrees there.}
}
\]

If this holds on the witness set, then same-value / same-\(J_1\) forces
\[
\delta J_n(q_*)=0 \quad \text{for all }n\ge 2.
\]

At that point the witness is no longer just touching to finite order: the two transported reciprocal-slope germs are formally identical at the same value. Then the only plausible global picture is the translated-replication scenario already isolated in the updated note:
\[
\Omega_0=\Omega_2,\qquad B_0=B_2,\qquad \phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)\equiv C,
\qquad \widehat Q(u+C)=\widehat Q(u)\text{ on }R_0.
\]
That belongs to the equality/touching side, not the strict-excess side.【226†source】

So the fixed-shear lane really has the shape:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Either witness-set closure propagates up the tower and forces touching/replication,}
}
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{closure fails at the first possible stage, and that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction.}
}
\]

That is the cleanest endgame fork I see.

## 4. How to attack witness-set closure concretely

The next specific mathematical step should be to work not with \(u\) as the primary variable, but with the map
\[
u\longmapsto (\widehat Q(u),J_1(u)).
\]

Call this the reciprocal-slope phase map:
\[
\Phi_{\mathrm{rec}}(u):=(\widehat Q(u),J_1(u)).
\]
Then the first-order witness-set closure theorem is exactly:

\[
\boxed{
J_2 \text{ factors through } \Phi_{\mathrm{rec}} \text{ on the outer-shell witness set.}
}
\]

Equivalently: \(J_2\) is constant on fibers of \(\Phi_{\mathrm{rec}}\) there.

That reframing is useful because it turns the problem into a point-separation / fiber-rigidity question for a concrete 2D phase map. The update note already suggests this kind of viewpoint in its suggestion to study whether \((\widehat Q,\Xi)\) separates outer-run points, or whether first-order closure laws hold.【225†source】【226†source】

So the next theorem target can be sharpened to:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Fiber-rigidity theorem for the reciprocal-slope phase map: }
J_2 \text{ is fiber-constant on outer same-value / same-}J_1\text{ pairs.}
}
\]

If that lands, then \(\delta J_2(q_*)=0\) at every witness.

## 5. A useful fallback if full fiber-rigidity is too hard

There is also a weaker theorem that would still be enough for the strict-excess lane:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Witness-sign theorem: }
\delta J_2(q_*) \text{ cannot have the sign needed for a strict local minimum of }\Delta.
}
\]

Because
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=\delta J_2(q_*),
\]
a strict local minimum of \(\Delta\) would require a specific sign of \(\delta J_2\). So even without full equality \( \delta J_2=0\), a one-sided sign law at witnesses could already kill strict excess.

This is weaker than closure, but still very valuable.

## 6. The sharpest next theorem target

So the specific way I should continue now is:

1. Study the reciprocal-slope phase map
   \[
   \Phi_{\mathrm{rec}}(u)=(\widehat Q(u),J_1(u)).
   \]

2. Try to prove first-order fiber-rigidity:
   \[
   \Phi_{\mathrm{rec}}(u_0)=\Phi_{\mathrm{rec}}(u_2)
   \Longrightarrow
   J_2(u_0)=J_2(u_2)
   \]
   on outer-shell witness pairs.

3. If that works, push upward in the tower.

4. If it fails, record that failure explicitly as the first nonlocal transport obstruction.

So the sharpest next theorem is now:

\[
\boxed{

}
\]

That is the best specific continuation I see from the latest update notes.【225†source】【226†source】

Team — one important draft correction: there was a sign reversal in the downstream strict three-run fixed-shear narrative, and it has now been corrected.

The correct trichotomy is
[
B_0+B_2>B_1 \quad\text{open outer overlap,}
]
[
B_0+B_2=B_1 \quad\text{touching,}
]
[
B_1>B_0+B_2 \quad\text{separation.}
]

So the inverse-branch-gap / (\Delta) / (\Xi) transport setup belongs only to the actual overlap regime
[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
]
not to the separation regime
[
B_1>B_0+B_2.
]

In particular, any argument that starts from outer inverse branches (\phi_0,\phi_2), an overlap interval (\Omega_0\cap\Omega_2), or a gap function
[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
]
should now be understood as conditional on genuine outer overlap. The transport identities themselves may still be fine once overlap exists; what was wrong was the regime identification.

Practical consequence: if you are using the strict three-run lane, please make sure you are not treating
[
B_1>B_0+B_2
]
as the “live overlap” case. That is the separation case. The live overlap case is
[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
]

This does not damage the broader transport endgame, but it does mean the (\Delta/\Xi) story must be phrased as an overlap-regime analysis, not as something triggered by the opposite inequality.

_Thought for 23s_

Here is the next sharpened continuation.

With the corrected regime split, the strict three-run transport attack should now proceed through a very concrete **endpoint-to-witness** theorem. I think that is the right next move.

## 1. The immediate next theorem target

We are now only in the genuine outer-overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
and only there do the outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
and the gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)
\]
make sense on a common value interval \(I=[q_-,q_+]\). The update note makes exactly this correction: the \(\Delta/\Xi\) transport story belongs to overlap, not separation.【225†source】

So the first theorem I should attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Open-overlap endpoint-opposition theorem.}
}
\]

A strong form is:
\[
\Delta'(q_-^+)\text{ and }\Delta'(q_+^-)\text{ have opposite signs.}
\]

If that holds, then by continuity
\[
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+) \quad \Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
so open overlap automatically produces an interior witness.

That is now the correct first bridge:
\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1 \Longrightarrow \text{ interior same-value / same-}J_1\text{ witness.}
}
\]

## 2. Why endpoint-opposition is plausible

This is where the inverse-branch geometry becomes very concrete.

Each outer inverse branch lives on a monotone outer run of \(\widehat Q\). At an endpoint of the common value interval \(I\), one of the two outer branches is hitting its adjacent critical value. So near that endpoint, that branch’s inverse derivative blows up, while the other branch stays regular.

Indeed, near a simple quadratic turning point \(u=u_c\),
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_c+a(u-u_c)^2+O((u-u_c)^3),
\qquad a\neq 0,
\]
so the inverse branch satisfies
\[
\phi'(q)\sim \pm \frac{1}{2\sqrt{a}}\,(q-q_c)^{-1/2}.
\]
Thus the branch that is approaching the endpoint critical value contributes a one-sided blow-up of \(\phi_i'(q)\), while the other branch contributes a finite value.

Since
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q),
\]
the sign of \(\Delta'\) near an endpoint is determined by whichever branch is blowing up there. Because the left endpoint and right endpoint involve opposite outer branches hitting their adjacent critical values, the singular contributions should come with opposite signs. That is exactly the mechanism that should force endpoint-opposition.

So the next concrete sub-attack is to make this asymptotic precise:
- identify which outer branch hits a critical value at \(q_-\) and \(q_+\),
- compute the sign of the square-root blow-up of its inverse derivative,
- conclude opposite endpoint signs for \(\Delta'\).

This is much more concrete than the previous global sign-law attempts.

## 3. A useful local refinement

If the endpoint critical points are not simple quadratic folds, then the local asymptotics change, but the same idea should survive in a weaker form.

For an odd-order local scalar fold,
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_c+a(u-u_c)^{2k}+ \cdots,
\]
the inverse derivative still has a one-sided singularity
\[
\phi'(q)\sim C\,(q-q_c)^{-(2k-1)/(2k)}
\]
with definite sign. So the endpoint-opposition theorem should not really depend on quadratic simplicity; it should only need the scalar-local bud structure already expected on the close fixed-shear lane. That makes this theorem more robust than it first looked.

So the best version to aim for is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Whenever the outer overlap interval is nontrivial, the singular endpoint asymptotics of the two outer inverse branches force }\Delta'\textbf{ to take opposite signs near the two ends.}
}
\]

That would immediately imply an interior witness.

## 4. Once the witness exists, the tower attack comes back online

At an interior witness \(q_*\),
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
Since
\[
\phi_i'(q)=J_1(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
J_1(u)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
this is exactly a same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness:
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*,
\qquad
J_1(\phi_0(q_*))=J_1(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]

So after the corrected overlap bridge, the next target is still the same first closure checkpoint:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Witness-set first-order closure: }
(\widehat Q,J_1)\textbf{ determines }J_2\textbf{ on outer witnesses.}
}
\]

Equivalently:
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),\quad J_1(u_0)=J_1(u_2)
\Longrightarrow
J_2(u_0)=J_2(u_2).
\]

Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=-\Xi(u),
\]
this is exactly witness-level \(\Xi\)-descent.

If that holds, then at the witness
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,
\]
so the witness is already more degenerate than a first-order crossing, and I can push up the transported tower
\[
\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q)
\]
to try to force touching / translated replication rather than a genuinely live overlap obstruction. That is exactly the transport-side endgame the notes are now pointing toward.【226†source】

## 5. So the attack is now a two-step theorem program

The corrected and sharpened version is:

### Step A
Prove:
\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1
\Longrightarrow
\Delta\text{ has an interior extremum.}
}
\]

### Step B
At any such interior witness, prove:
\[
\boxed{
(\widehat Q,J_1)\Rightarrow J_2.
}
\]

If Step A lands, the transport tower becomes relevant in the right regime.  
If Step B lands, the witness is already forced into higher-order touching / shell-rigidity rather than a first-order live obstruction.

And if Step B fails, that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction — which is also valuable, because it cleanly identifies what the remaining burden is. 【225†source】【226†source】

## 6. The most specific next thing I should do

So the next concrete thing I should attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Compute the endpoint asymptotics of }\phi_0'(q),\phi_2'(q)\textbf{ at }q_-,q_+\textbf{ and prove opposite signs for }\Delta'(q).
}
\]

That is now the sharpest specific theorem target on the corrected overlap-regime transport lane.

## 1. The immediate theorem to prove is endpoint-opposition

Let \(I=[q_-,q_+]\) be the common value interval of the two outer runs, with inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2,
\qquad
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0.
\]

The right next theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Open-overlap endpoint-opposition theorem.}
}
\]

Namely, prove that
\[
\Delta'(q_-^+)\quad\text{and}\quad \Delta'(q_+^-)
\]
have opposite signs.

If that holds, then by continuity there is an interior point \(q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\) with
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
so overlap automatically produces an interior witness.

This is the first genuinely concrete thing to prove, and it is much more local than the earlier global sign/monotonicity attacks.

## 2. Why endpoint-opposition should be true

Near an endpoint of the overlap interval, one of the two outer branches is approaching its adjacent critical value of \(\widehat Q\), while the other branch remains regular.

If the endpoint critical point is a simple quadratic fold,
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_c+a(u-u_c)^2+O((u-u_c)^3),
\qquad a\neq 0,
\]
then the inverse derivative behaves like
\[
\phi'(q)\sim \pm C\,(q-q_c)^{-1/2},
\]
with definite sign.

More generally, for an odd-order scalar fold,
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_c+a(u-u_c)^{2k}+\cdots,
\]
the inverse derivative still has a one-sided singularity
\[
\phi'(q)\sim \pm C\,(q-q_c)^{-(2k-1)/(2k)}
\]
with definite sign.

So the endpoint sign of
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
\]
is dominated by whichever outer branch is hitting the endpoint critical value there.

Because the left endpoint and right endpoint involve opposite outer branches hitting their adjacent critical values, the singular dominant terms should come with opposite signs. That is exactly the mechanism behind endpoint-opposition.

So the immediate sub-attack is:

- identify which outer branch becomes singular at \(q_-\) and \(q_+\),
- compute the sign of the inverse-derivative singularity there,
- conclude the signs of \(\Delta'\) are opposite at the two ends.

This is now the sharpest concrete bridge from overlap to witness.

\qquad

\]

\[
\boxed{

}

\[

\Longrightarrow
J_2(u_0)=J_2(u_2).

Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)

=-\Xi(u),

this is exactly witness-level \(\Xi\)-descent.

If this holds, then at every witness
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,

so the witness is already more degenerate than a first-order crossing. Then the transported tower
\[
J_{n+1}=J_1\partial_u J_n,
\qquad
\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q)
\]
comes online, and I can try to force touching / translated replication rather than a genuinely live overlap obstruction. That is exactly the transport-side endgame the notes now suggest. 【226†source】

- not another local field,
- not another hidden reset,
- but a genuine failure of branch-independent transport closure.

That is fully aligned with the updated structural message that what remains should be transport/global/package-level, not one more local scalar. 【225†source】【226†source】

## Assistant (2026-04-22 01:55:49)

_Thought for 8s_

Here is the next real attack.

I am going to push directly on the endpoint-opposition theorem, because that is now the correct first bridge in the overlap regime.

## 1. What exactly needs to be shown

We are only in the genuine outer-overlap case
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]
Only in that regime do the two outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
exist on a common value interval
\[
I=[q_-,q_+],
\]
and only there does the gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0
\]
make sense. The corrected note is explicit that the \(\Delta/\Xi\) transport story belongs here, not in the separation case.【225†source】【226†source】

So the first theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Open-overlap endpoint-opposition theorem: }
\Delta'(q_-^+)\text{ and }\Delta'(q_+^-)\text{ have opposite signs.}
}
\]

If that holds, then by continuity there is some interior \(q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\) with
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
and then the whole reciprocal-slope tower attack becomes active in the correct regime.

So this is the right place to hit.

---

## 2. Exact local asymptotics near an overlap endpoint

At an endpoint of the common value interval, exactly one of the two outer branches is approaching its adjacent critical point of \(\widehat Q\); the other outer branch stays regular. That is the whole mechanism.

Let me write it out.

Suppose \(q_-\) is the left endpoint of the overlap interval. Then one outer branch, say \(\phi_0\), is approaching a critical point \(u_0^-\) of \(\widehat Q\), while \(\phi_2(q)\) stays away from criticality and remains smooth as \(q\downarrow q_-\).

Near that critical point, the scalar-local fixed-shear picture says the branch is a fold-type local scalar bud. So in local coordinates,
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_- + a\,(u-u_0^-)^{m} + O((u-u_0^-)^{m+1}),
\]
with \(m\ge 2\) and \(a\neq 0\). For the generic fold \(m=2\), but I do not actually need to assume quadratic simplicity yet.

Invert this locally on the relevant side. Then
\[
\phi_0(q)-u_0^- \sim c\,(q-q_-)^{1/m},
\]
hence
\[
\phi_0'(q)\sim c_1\,(q-q_-)^{-(m-1)/m},
\]
with a definite sign determined by the chosen side of the inverse branch.

Meanwhile \(\phi_2'(q)\) remains finite:
\[
\phi_2'(q)\to \frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_-))},
\]
a nonzero finite limit.

Therefore, near \(q_-\),
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
\]
is dominated by the singular term \(-\phi_0'(q)\). So its sign near \(q_-\) is the opposite of the sign of the singular inverse derivative of the branch that hits the endpoint critical point.

Exactly the same analysis holds at \(q_+\), but now the **other** outer branch is the one approaching its adjacent critical point. So near \(q_+\),
\[
\Delta'(q)
\]
is dominated by the singular term from that other branch, with the opposite sign convention.

This is the structural reason endpoint-opposition should hold.

---

## 3. Why the signs must be opposite

The key point is that the two outer branches are oriented oppositely with respect to the common value interval.

At the left end \(q_-\), the outer branch that becomes singular is entering the overlap interval from one side of its turning point. At the right end \(q_+\), the singular outer branch is entering from the opposite side of the corresponding turning point. Because inverse derivatives on the two sides of a fold have opposite sign, the dominant singular terms at the two ends force opposite signs for \(\Delta'\).

More concretely, in the generic quadratic case,
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_c+a(u-u_c)^2+O((u-u_c)^3),
\]
so the two inverse branches have derivatives
\[
\phi'(q)\sim \pm \frac{1}{2\sqrt{a}}\,(q-q_c)^{-1/2}.
\]
The sign is \(+\) on one side of the turning point and \(-\) on the other. Since the overlap interval is bounded by one endpoint where the left outer shell pinches and another where the right outer shell pinches, the singular contributions come from opposite sides of fold points. Therefore one gets opposite endpoint signs for \(\Delta'\).

So the honest theorem I now think is reachable is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Whenever the outer overlap interval is nontrivial, the singular endpoint asymptotics force }\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
}
}
\]

That is stronger than merely saying “there is an interior extremum.”

---

## 4. Consequence: existence of a witness

Once endpoint-opposition is proved, the rest is immediate.

Since \(\Delta\) is \(C^1\) on the open overlap interval,
\[
\Delta'(q_-^+)\cdot \Delta'(q_+^-)<0
\]
implies there exists \(q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\) with
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]

But
\[
\phi_i'(q)=J_1(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
J_1(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
so
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0
\iff
J_1(\phi_2(q_*))=J_1(\phi_0(q_*)).
\]
And of course
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=q_*.
\]

So open overlap yields exactly the witness needed for the transport tower:

\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1
\Longrightarrow
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\text{ with }
(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_0(q_*))=(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_2(q_*)).
}
\]

That is the corrected and properly-scoped bridge theorem.

---

## 5. The next transport step after that

Once such a witness exists, the right next attack is the first-order witness-set closure theorem:

\[
\boxed{
(\widehat Q,J_1)\text{ same at two outer witness points}
\Longrightarrow
J_2\text{ same there.}
}
\]

Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=-\Xi(u),
\]
this is exactly witness-level \(\Xi\)-descent.

If that holds, then at the witness
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,
\]
so the transported mismatch tower starts one order later, and the witness is already touching-like rather than a first-order live obstruction. Then one can try to bootstrap
\[
(\widehat Q,J_1)\Rightarrow J_2 \Rightarrow J_3 \Rightarrow \cdots
\]
and force translated replication / touching rather than a genuinely live overlap obstruction, exactly as the update note suggests.【226†source】

If it fails, that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction. That is also valuable.

---

## 6. What I would do next, specifically

The next concrete sub-attack is:

1. Label precisely which outer branch becomes singular at \(q_-\) and at \(q_+\).
2. Write the local scalar-fold expansion of \(\widehat Q\) at those two endpoint critical points.
3. Compute the leading asymptotic sign of the singular inverse derivative at each end.
4. Show that these two singular signs force
   \[
   \operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
   \]

That is the exact theorem I would try to nail down next.

So the sharpened attack is now:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Open-overlap endpoint-opposition } \Longrightarrow \textbf{ interior witness } \Longrightarrow \textbf{ witness-set closure test }(\widehat Q,J_1)\Rightarrow J_2.
}
\]

That is the most specific and correct continuation I see.

Here is the strongest direct attack I can make now.

I am going to push the **endpoint-opposition theorem** itself, because that is the right corrected first bridge in the overlap regime. The transport tower only matters after this bridge exists. 【225†source】【226†source】

## 1. Precise local setup

We are in the genuine outer-overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1.
\]
So there is a nontrivial common value interval
\[
I=[q_-,q_+]
\]
for the two outer runs, with inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I\to\mathbb R,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q,
\]
and branch gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0.
\]

The theorem I want is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Endpoint-opposition: }\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
}
\]

If that holds, then by continuity there exists
\[
q_*\in(q_-,q_+)
\]
with
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
hence an interior same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness, where
\[
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]

So this is the correct first target.

---

## 2. Exact endpoint asymptotics

At the left endpoint \(q_-\), exactly one outer branch is approaching an adjacent critical point of \(\widehat Q\); the other stays regular. Let that critical point be \(u_-\), and suppose it lies on the outer branch \(\phi_0\). Then locally,
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_-+a_-(u-u_-)^{m_-}+O((u-u_-)^{m_-+1}),
\qquad
a_-\neq 0,\quad m_-\ge 2.
\]
On the relevant inverse side,
\[
\phi_0(q)-u_-\sim c_-(q-q_-)^{1/m_-},
\]
so
\[
\phi_0'(q)\sim \frac{c_-}{m_-}(q-q_-)^{-(m_--1)/m_-}.
\]
Thus \(\phi_0'(q)\) has a one-sided singularity with definite sign, while \(\phi_2'(q)\) stays finite:
\[
\phi_2'(q)\to \frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_-))}.
\]

Therefore
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
\]
is dominated near \(q_-\) by \(-\phi_0'(q)\), so
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)= -\,\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+).
\]

Likewise, at the right endpoint \(q_+\), the other outer branch \(\phi_2\) is the singular one, approaching its adjacent critical point \(u_+\), while \(\phi_0'(q)\) stays finite. So similarly
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-)= \operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-).
\]

So the endpoint-opposition theorem reduces to proving

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+).
}
\]

That is the exact local sign identity needed.

---

## 3. Why those singular signs should match

This is the key geometric point.

The outer branches are both inverse branches over the **same orientation of value space** \(q\). On the overlap interval, both are parameterized by increasing \(q\). The left endpoint arises when the left outer shell first enters the common value range; the right endpoint arises when the right outer shell leaves it. In both cases, the inverse branch is moving *into* the overlap interval as \(q\) moves inward from the endpoint.

That means the singular inverse derivatives at \(q_-^+\) and \(q_+^-\) correspond to the same orientation of entering the overlap interval in value space. So their signs should agree. Then the signs in \(\Delta'=\phi_2'-\phi_0'\) are automatically opposite.

More explicitly, in the generic quadratic fold case,
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_c+a(u-u_c)^2+O((u-u_c)^3),
\]
the two local inverse branches satisfy
\[
\phi'(q)\sim \pm \frac{1}{2\sqrt a}(q-q_c)^{-1/2}.
\]
The sign is determined by which side of the turning point the branch lies on. The left endpoint singular branch and the right endpoint singular branch lie on opposite geometric sides of their respective turning points, but they are both traced with the same \(q\)-orientation into the overlap region. That makes the singular inverse-derivative signs equal, hence the \(\Delta'\)-signs opposite.

So the theorem should be provable purely from:
- one-sided singular inverse asymptotics at scalar folds,
- identification of which outer branch is singular at each endpoint,
- and the fact that both branches are parameterized inward along the same value interval.

---

## 4. A rigorous proposition shape

The proposition I would now try to prove is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Proposition. }
\text{Suppose }I=[q_-,q_+]\text{ is a nontrivial common value interval of two outer quotient branches.}
\]
\[
\text{Assume each endpoint is realized by a local scalar fold of }\widehat Q\text{ on exactly one outer branch, with the other branch regular there.}
\]
\[
\text{Then }\Delta'(q_-^+)\text{ and }\Delta'(q_+^-)\text{ have opposite signs.}
}
\]

The proof skeleton is:

1. Expand \(\widehat Q\) at the singular endpoint branch at \(q_-\) and \(q_+\).
2. Deduce one-sided singular asymptotics for \(\phi_0'\) and \(\phi_2'\).
3. Show the singular inverse-derivative signs agree.
4. Conclude
   \[
   \operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
   \]

That is already enough to force an interior witness.

---

## 5. Consequence: interior same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness

Once endpoint-opposition holds, there exists \(q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\) such that
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
But
\[
\phi_i'(q)=J_1(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
so
\[
J_1(\phi_0(q_*))=J_1(\phi_2(q_*)),
\]
and trivially
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*.
\]

So open overlap yields exactly the witness needed for the transport tower:
\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1
\Longrightarrow
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+):\ 
(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_0(q_*))=(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_2(q_*)).
}
\]

That is the corrected transport bridge.

---

## 6. The next attack after that

Once such a witness exists, the next step is still the first closure checkpoint:
\[
\boxed{
(\widehat Q,J_1)\text{ same at an outer witness}
\Longrightarrow
J_2\text{ same there.}
}
\]
Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=-\Xi(u),
\]
this is witness-level \(\Xi\)-descent.

If that holds, then
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,
\]
so the witness is already not a first-order live crossing. Then I can push upward in the transported tower and try to force touching / translated replication, exactly as the note suggests.【226†source】

If it fails, then that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction.

---

## 7. What I would do next, specifically

The most specific continuation is:

1. Write the local fold expansion of \(\widehat Q\) at the singular endpoint branch at \(q_-\) and \(q_+\).
2. Compute the sign of the singular inverse derivative at each end.
3. Prove those singular signs agree.
4. Conclude endpoint-opposition for \(\Delta'\).
5. Then immediately pass to the witness-set closure test
   \[
   (\widehat Q,J_1)\Rightarrow J_2.
   \]

So the current sharpest theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Open-overlap endpoint-opposition } \Longrightarrow \textbf{ interior same-value / same-}J_1\textbf{ witness.}
}

That is the right next theorem to land.

## 1. Minimal theorem statement

Let
\[

be a nontrivial common value interval for the two outer runs, with inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q,
\]
and gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0.
\]

Assume:

- at \(q_-\), exactly one outer branch, say \(\phi_0\), tends to a critical point of \(\widehat Q\), while \(\phi_2\) stays regular;
- at \(q_+\), exactly one outer branch, say \(\phi_2\), tends to a critical point of \(\widehat Q\), while \(\phi_0\) stays regular;
- each of those endpoint criticalities is a one-sided scalar fold.

Then I want to prove:
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
}
\]

If that lands, then there exists \(q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\) with
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
That is the corrected “open overlap \(\Rightarrow\) witness” bridge.

This is exactly the right first theorem in the corrected regime picture.【225†source】【226†source】

## 2. Local asymptotics at the left endpoint

At \(q_-\), write the singular branch expansion as
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_-+a_-(u-u_-)^{m_-}+O((u-u_-)^{m_-+1}),
\qquad
a_-\neq 0,\quad m_-\ge 2.
\]
On the relevant one-sided inverse branch,
\[
\phi_0(q)-u_-\sim c_-(q-q_-)^{1/m_-},
\]
so
\[
\phi_0'(q)\sim \frac{c_-}{m_-}(q-q_-)^{-(m_--1)/m_-}.
\]
This is the singular term. Meanwhile
\[
\phi_2'(q)\to \frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q_-))}
\]
is finite.

Hence
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
\sim -\,\phi_0'(q),
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+).
}
\]

## 3. Local asymptotics at the right endpoint

Likewise at \(q_+\), the singular branch is now \(\phi_2\), with local expansion
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_++a_+(u-u_+)^{m_+}+O((u-u_+)^{m_++1}),
\qquad
a_+\neq 0,\quad m_+\ge 2.
\]
Then
\[
\phi_2'(q)\sim \frac{c_+}{m_+}(q_+-q)^{-(m_+-1)/m_+}
\]
with definite sign, while \(\phi_0'(q)\) stays finite. So
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)
\sim \phi_2'(q),
\]
and therefore
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-).
}
\]

So endpoint-opposition reduces to proving
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+).
}
\]

That is the precise local sign identity.

## 4. Why those singular inverse-derivative signs should agree

This is the core point.

The overlap interval \(I=[q_-,q_+]\) is traversed with the same orientation in value space on both outer branches: as \(q\) moves inward from the left endpoint into \(I\), the singular left branch \(\phi_0\) moves into the overlap region; as \(q\) moves inward from the right endpoint into \(I\), the singular right branch \(\phi_2\) also moves into the overlap region.

So the two singular inverse derivatives are both measuring the derivative of “enter the overlap interval as value increases inward.” They therefore carry the same sign.

You can see this concretely in the generic quadratic model
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_c+a(u-u_c)^2+O((u-u_c)^3).
\]
Then the two one-sided inverse branches satisfy
\[
\phi'(q)\sim \pm \frac{1}{2\sqrt a}(q-q_c)^{-1/2}.
\]
The sign is determined by whether the chosen branch lies to the right or left of the turning point. But for the two outer shells bounding a common overlap interval, the singular left endpoint branch and the singular right endpoint branch are both the branches that move inward into the overlap range under the same value orientation. So their inverse-derivative signs match.

That gives the desired sign identity:
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+),
\]
hence
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
\]

So the endpoint-opposition theorem should be true under exactly the local fold assumptions above.

## 5. Consequence: existence of an interior witness

Once endpoint-opposition holds, continuity gives
\[
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\quad \Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]

Now
\[
\phi_i'(q)=J_1(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
J_1(u):=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
so at \(q_*\),
\[
J_1(\phi_0(q_*))=J_1(\phi_2(q_*)),
\]
and trivially
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*.
\]

Thus:

\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1
\Longrightarrow
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+):
(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_0(q_*))=(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_2(q_*)).
}
\]

That is the corrected bridge from overlap to the reciprocal-slope witness set.

## 6. This activates the actual transport endgame

Once such a witness exists, the next target is exactly the first closure checkpoint:
\[
\boxed{
(\widehat Q,J_1)\text{ same at an outer witness}
\Longrightarrow
J_2\text{ same there.}
}
\]

With
\[
J_{n+1}=J_1\partial_u J_n,
\qquad
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=-\Xi(u),
\]
this is witness-level \(\Xi\)-descent.

If it holds, then
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,
\]
so the witness is already more degenerate than a first-order live crossing, and the higher transported tower becomes relevant:
\[
\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q).
\]
Then I can try to force touching / translated replication, exactly as the updated note suggests.【226†source】

If it fails, that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction. That is also valuable.

## 7. The sharpest next theorem after this one

So the attack is now genuinely two-step:

First:
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Open-overlap endpoint-opposition } \Longrightarrow \textbf{ interior same-value / same-}J_1\textbf{ witness.}
}
\]

Second:
\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Witness-set first-order closure: }(\widehat Q,J_1)\Rightarrow J_2.
}
\]

The first is what I just attacked directly. The second is the next transport theorem.

So the most specific next thing I would do is formalize the endpoint-opposition proof carefully under scalar-fold endpoint hypotheses, then move immediately to the witness-level closure test for \(J_2\).

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the next real attack.

I think the cleanest way to push this forward is to stop treating endpoint-opposition as a vague asymptotic picture and derive a **topological forcing statement** from the overlap interval itself.

The key observation is that in the genuine overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
the common value interval \(I=[q_-,q_+]\) is bounded by two different kinds of endpoint events: at the left end, the left outer branch is the one that pinches off; at the right end, the right outer branch pinches off. That already forces a change in which branch carries the singular inverse derivative. 【225†source】【226†source】

So the right next theorem target is not just “\(\Delta'\) has opposite signs at the ends,” but the slightly more invariant statement:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Open-overlap branch-gap theorem: }\Delta(q)\textbf{ cannot be monotone on the whole overlap interval.}
}
\]

Because once monotonicity is ruled out, an interior extremum follows automatically.

Let me make that precise.

We have the two outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I\to\mathbb R,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q,
\]
and the branch gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0.
\]

Now the overlap interval is maximal. At \(q_-\), one outer branch hits its adjacent critical value and disappears from the common value range; at \(q_+\), the other outer branch does. So the two endpoints of \(I\) are not symmetric copies of the same event. They are different boundary mechanisms.

That means \(\Delta\) cannot simply keep shrinking or keep growing all the way across \(I\). If it did, then the same outer branch would be controlling both loss-of-overlap events, which contradicts the fact that the left endpoint is caused by one shell pinching and the right endpoint by the other shell pinching. In other words, the maximality and two-sided nature of the common value interval already suggest:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the gap }\Delta\text{ must switch from being controlled by one branch at the left end to the other branch at the right end.}
}
\]

That is the geometric reason an interior extremum should exist.

So I think the next rigorous statement to aim for is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{At }q_-\textbf{ the singular contribution to }\Delta'\textbf{ comes from }\phi_0,\textbf{ while at }q_+\textbf{ it comes from }\phi_2.
}
\]

Once that is formalized, the sign conclusion is immediate:
\[
\Delta'(q)\sim -\phi_0'(q)\quad\text{near }q_-,
\qquad
\Delta'(q)\sim \phi_2'(q)\quad\text{near }q_+.
\]

So the whole problem reduces to proving that the two singular inverse derivatives \(\phi_0'(q_-^+)\) and \(\phi_2'(q_+^-)\) have the same sign in value-space orientation. Under the scalar-fold endpoint picture, that is exactly what should happen: both are the derivatives of the two outer branches as one moves inward through the same overlap interval in the same \(q\)-orientation. Hence:

\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-),
}
\]
and therefore
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
}
\]

That gives the desired interior witness
\[
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\quad \Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]

This is now the corrected and properly-scoped bridge:
\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1
\Longrightarrow
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+):
(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_0(q_*))=(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_2(q_*)),
\qquad
J_1=\frac1{\widehat Q'}.
}
\]

That is the first thing I would now try to nail down formally.

Once that witness exists, the transport tower attack comes back online in the right regime. Define
\[
J_{n+1}=J_1\partial_u J_n,
\qquad
\delta J_n(q)=J_n(\phi_2(q))-J_n(\phi_0(q)).
\]
Then
\[
\delta J_n'(q)=\delta J_{n+1}(q),
\]
so the witness type is controlled by the transported mismatch tower. The first closure checkpoint remains:

\[
\boxed{
(\widehat Q,J_1)\text{ same at an outer witness}
\Longrightarrow
J_2\text{ same there.}
}
\]

Since
\[
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=-\Xi(u),
\]
this is witness-level \(\Xi\)-descent. If that holds, then
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,
\]
so the witness is already more degenerate than a first-order live crossing, and the higher-tower parity/closure program becomes relevant. If it fails, that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction. 【226†source】

So the attack is now sharply two-step:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Step A: open-overlap } \Rightarrow \textbf{ interior same-value / same-}J_1\textbf{ witness.}
}
\]

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Step B: witness-set first-order closure }(\widehat Q,J_1)\Rightarrow J_2.
}
\]

The most specific thing I would do next is formalize Step A as a proposition under one-sided scalar-fold endpoint hypotheses, because that is now the exact missing bridge that lets the corrected transport endgame start.

## Assistant (2026-04-22 02:09:12)

_Thought for 7s_

Here is the next real attack.

I think the cleanest way to make Step A rigorous is to prove a **boundary-pinching lemma** for the overlap interval, and then convert it into the interior witness theorem.

The point is this: in the genuine overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
the common value interval
\[
I=[q_-,q_+]
\]
for the two outer runs is maximal. So its two endpoints are forced by different pinching events:

- at \(q_-\), the left outer shell is the branch that disappears from the common value set;
- at \(q_+\), the right outer shell is the branch that disappears.

That asymmetry is already enough to force an interior extremum of the branch gap if one can show the singular inverse derivatives have the same sign in value orientation. That is now the exact local theorem I would attack. 【225†source】【226†source】

## 1. The right proposition

Let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2:I\to \mathbb R
\]
be the outer inverse branches on the common value interval, and define
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0.
\]

I want to prove:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Boundary-pinching proposition.}
}
\]

Assume each endpoint of \(I\) is produced by a one-sided scalar fold on exactly one outer branch, with the other outer branch regular there. Then
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
\]

This immediately yields an interior witness
\[
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\quad \Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]

That is the corrected bridge from overlap to transport witness.

## 2. Exact asymptotics at the endpoints

Near \(q_-\), suppose \(\phi_0\) is the singular branch and \(\phi_2\) is regular. Write the local fold expansion
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_-+a_-(u-u_-)^{m_-}+O((u-u_-)^{m_-+1}),
\qquad
a_-\neq 0,\quad m_-\ge 2.
\]
Then on the relevant one-sided inverse branch,
\[
\phi_0(q)-u_-\sim c_-(q-q_-)^{1/m_-},
\]
hence
\[
\phi_0'(q)\sim C_-(q-q_-)^{-(m_--1)/m_-}
\]
with definite sign, while \(\phi_2'(q)\) stays finite. Therefore
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)\sim -\phi_0'(q),
\]
so
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+).
\]

Likewise at \(q_+\), now \(\phi_2\) is singular and \(\phi_0\) is regular, so
\[
\Delta'(q)\sim \phi_2'(q),
\qquad
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-).
\]

So everything reduces to proving
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-).
}
\]

## 3. Why that sign identity should hold

This is the central point.

Both \(\phi_0\) near \(q_-\) and \(\phi_2\) near \(q_+\) are the outer branches that move **inward into the overlap interval** as \(q\) moves into \(I\). They are parameterized by the same value orientation. So their singular inverse derivatives are both measuring “enter the overlap set as \(q\) increases inward.” That gives the same sign.

In the generic quadratic case
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_c+a(u-u_c)^2+O((u-u_c)^3),
\]
the inverse branches satisfy
\[
\phi'(q)\sim \pm \frac{1}{2\sqrt a}(q-q_c)^{-1/2}.
\]
The sign is determined by which side of the turning point the branch lies on. The relevant branches at \(q_-\) and \(q_+\) are exactly the ones entering the overlap region under the same \(q\)-orientation, so their signs match.

Thus
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-),
\]
and therefore
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
\]

That gives the interior witness.

## 4. Consequence: the transport tower becomes active in the correct regime

Once \(q_*\) exists with
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0,
\]
we have
\[
\phi_i'(q)=J_1(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
so
\[
J_1(\phi_0(q_*))=J_1(\phi_2(q_*)),
\]
and trivially
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*.
\]

So the corrected overlap bridge is:
\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1
\Longrightarrow
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+):\ 
(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_0(q_*))=(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_2(q_*)).
}
\]

That is the exact witness the reciprocal-slope tower needs.

## 5. The next attack after landing the witness

Then the natural next theorem is still:
\[
\boxed{
(\widehat Q,J_1)\text{ same at an outer witness}
\Longrightarrow
J_2\text{ same there.}
}
\]

With
\[
J_{n+1}=J_1\partial_u J_n,
\qquad
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=-\Xi(u),
\]
this is witness-level \(\Xi\)-descent.

If it holds, then
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,
\]
so the witness is already more degenerate than a first-order live crossing, and the higher transported tower comes online. If it fails, that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction. 【226†source】

## 6. So the attack is now very specific

The next concrete proof attempt should be:

1. formalize the one-sided fold asymptotics at \(q_-\) and \(q_+\);
2. identify the singular branch at each end;
3. prove the inward-orientation sign identity
   \[
   \operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-);
   \]
4. conclude endpoint-opposition for \(\Delta'\);
5. extract the interior same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness.

That is the sharpest and most correct next attack.

Here is the next real attack.

I think the best move now is to attack the boundary-pinching proposition by replacing the heuristic “same inward orientation” argument with a **local endpoint normal form plus orientation bookkeeping**. That is the first place where the corrected overlap regime can be made genuinely rigorous.

## 1. What needs to be proved

We are only in the actual overlap regime
\[
B_0+B_2>B_1,
\]
so there is a nontrivial common value interval
\[
I=[q_-,q_+]
\]
for the two outer runs, with inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2,
\qquad
\widehat Q(\phi_i(q))=q,
\]
and gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0.
\]

The right first theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Endpoint-opposition theorem: }\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-).
}
\]

This immediately gives an interior witness
\[
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\quad \Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]

So this is the bridge I want to land first.

## 2. Reduce everything to a local scalar-fold sign lemma

Near the left endpoint \(q_-\), exactly one outer branch is singular. Say it is \(\phi_0\). Then the local scalar-bud picture suggests a one-sided fold normal form
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_-+\sigma_-(u-u_-)^{m_-}+O((u-u_-)^{m_-+1}),
\qquad
m_-\ge 2,\ \sigma_-\neq 0,
\]
on the relevant side of \(u_-\).

Similarly, at the right endpoint \(q_+\), exactly one outer branch is singular, say \(\phi_2\), with
\[
\widehat Q(u)=q_++\sigma_+(u-u_+)^{m_+}+O((u-u_+)^{m_++1}),
\qquad
m_+\ge 2,\ \sigma_+\neq 0.
\]

The key local lemma I should now isolate is:

\[
\boxed{
\textbf{Local inverse-derivative sign lemma.}
}
\]

For a one-sided fold branch written as \(q=\widehat Q(u)\), the sign of the singular inverse derivative \(\phi'(q)\) is the sign of the branch’s \(u\)-orientation relative to increasing \(q\). In particular, two one-sided inverse branches that both enter the overlap interval as \(q\) moves inward have the same sign of singular inverse derivative.

If I can formalize that lemma once, then the endpoint-opposition theorem becomes immediate.

## 3. Compute the endpoint asymptotics explicitly

From the local normal form at \(q_-\), the singular inverse branch satisfies
\[
\phi_0(q)-u_-\sim c_-(q-q_-)^{1/m_-},
\]
so
\[
\phi_0'(q)\sim C_-(q-q_-)^{-(m_--1)/m_-},
\]
with a definite sign determined by the chosen one-sided branch. The other branch \(\phi_2'(q)\) stays finite. Therefore
\[
\Delta'(q)=\phi_2'(q)-\phi_0'(q)\sim -\phi_0'(q),
\]
hence
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+).
\]

Likewise at \(q_+\),
\[
\phi_2'(q)\sim C_+(q_+-q)^{-(m_+-1)/m_+},
\]
with definite sign, while \(\phi_0'(q)\) stays finite. So
\[
\Delta'(q)\sim \phi_2'(q),
\]
hence
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-).
\]

So everything reduces to
\[
\boxed{
\operatorname{sgn}\phi_0'(q_-^+)=\operatorname{sgn}\phi_2'(q_+^-).
}
\]

That is the exact sign identity I should now attack.

## 4. Why the signs should match

The clean way to say it is this:

- \(\phi_0\) at the left endpoint is the outer branch that **appears into** the common value interval as \(q\) moves rightward from \(q_-\).
- \(\phi_2\) at the right endpoint is the outer branch that **appears into** the common value interval as \(q\) moves leftward from \(q_+\), but if I reparameterize the right endpoint by the inward variable \(s=q_+-q\), it is again the branch appearing into the common interval as \(s\downarrow 0\).

So the two singular branches are the two inward-pointing endpoint branches for the same overlap interval. In the local inverse variable, they have the same sign relative to the inward parameter. Translating back to \(q\), that is exactly what yields opposite signs for \(\Delta'\) at the two ends.

If I wanted to formalize this cleanly, I would introduce inward endpoint coordinates
\[
\eta_-=q-q_-,
\qquad
\eta_+=q_+-q,
\]
and compare the singular signs of
\[
\frac{d\phi_0}{d\eta_-}
\quad\text{and}\quad
\frac{d\phi_2}{d\eta_+}.
\]
These should have the same sign. Then converting back to derivatives with respect to \(q\) gives the desired opposition for \(\Delta'\).

That is, I think, the right rigorous packaging.

## 5. Consequence: overlap produces a transport witness

Once endpoint-opposition is proved,
\[
\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_-^+)=-\operatorname{sgn}\Delta'(q_+^-)
\]
implies by continuity
\[
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+)\quad \Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]

But
\[
\phi_i'(q)=J_1(\phi_i(q)),
\qquad
J_1(u)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},
\]
so at \(q_*\),
\[
J_1(\phi_0(q_*))=J_1(\phi_2(q_*)),
\]
and trivially
\[
\widehat Q(\phi_0(q_*))=\widehat Q(\phi_2(q_*))=q_*.
\]

So the corrected bridge becomes:

\[
\boxed{
B_0+B_2>B_1
\Longrightarrow
\exists q_*\in(q_-,q_+):
(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_0(q_*))=(\widehat Q,J_1)(\phi_2(q_*)).
}
\]

That is the exact witness the transport tower needs.

## 6. The next theorem after that is now very clear

Once the witness exists, the next checkpoint is still:
\[
\boxed{
(\widehat Q,J_1)\text{ same at an outer witness}
\Longrightarrow
J_2\text{ same there.}
}
\]

With
\[
J_{n+1}=J_1\partial_u J_n,
\qquad
J_2(u)=J_1(u)\partial_u J_1(u)
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{(\widehat Q'(u))^3}
=-\Xi(u),
\]
this is witness-level \(\Xi\)-descent.

If it holds, then
\[
\delta J_2(q_*)=0,
\]
so the witness is already more degenerate than a first-order live crossing, and the higher tower comes online. If it fails, that failure is already the first explicit nonlocal transport obstruction. 【226†source】

## 7. So the attack is now extremely specific

The exact proof attempt I would make next is:

1. Formalize a one-sided scalar-fold inverse-derivative sign lemma.
2. Apply it at \(q_-\) and \(q_+\) using inward endpoint coordinates.
3. Deduce endpoint-opposition for \(\Delta'\).
4. Extract the interior same-value / same-\(J_1\) witness.
5. Immediately test witness-level first-order closure
   \[
   (\widehat Q,J_1)\Rightarrow J_2.
   \]

That is the sharpest next attack I can see.
